With a prefatory notice by “Rolf Boldrewood.”

are not less true than terrible I take it upon myself to affirm.

This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief

He has devoted a life- time to the study of
Nervous Complaints.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may be eradi-
cated; with these widely recognised qualification!
combined with unswerving faithfulness to hij
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practico
embraces diseases which probably cause more un
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
DO YOU SUFFER
from headaches, drowsiness during the day, circles
round the eyes, giddiness, irregularity of the
bowels, specks before the eyes, bashfulness, dis-
contentedness, weakness or pain in the back, liver
or kidney complaints, buzzing in the ears and
head, pimples, a fear that something dreadful is
about to happen, weak stomach, yellow or blood-
shot eyes, constipation, impure blood, hasty,
uncontrollable temper, hacking cough, nervous-

fectly well, its general information and prescrip-

to health, even though his case has been previously

One Pound is Sufficient to Send
for remedies necessary to cure any ordinary com-
plaint. Should you require medical treatment, as
procrastination is sometimes dangerous, it would
be advisable for you to sit down quietly in your

Beginning the Year.
(FOR THE BULLETIN. )
He lay on a moth-eaten mattress, thrown upon
the floor of a rat-eaten house in a suburban lane.
And she —the wife upon whom he had not set
eyes for five years —sat by his side, waiting for
him to come to his senses. For the fires of fever
burnt fiercely in his breast, and his life had
almost flickered out.
The tawdry blind flapped against the window,
as though the hand of Someone waiting outside
were stirring it. And the wind blew the flame of
the guttering candle to' and fro, and moaned
around the head of the dying man as though it
came from an unseen world, and whispered in his
ear.
He babbled in his delirium of many things.
The name of the wife he had deserted came often
to his lips. And then the woman by his side
would cover her wasted face with a no less wasted
hand, and silently rock herself in the shadow.
But more frequently he talked of gullies in the
ranges, where the beds of creeks glittered with
gold, and where thirst-tortured travellers, braving
the dangers of the bush by night and day, stumbled
along and threw themselves upon their knees,
dipping their arms into the golden stream, only to
groan despairingly when their hands came up full
of flakes of mica ! Always the same, the Dream
of Fortune and the Awakening.
It was in the pause after one of these cries of
despair that he came back to consciousness.
“ Where am I ? ” he asked faintly.
The woman, leaning back in the shadow,
answered him.
“ Who is that ? ” he asked again.
The woman came out of the shadow, and the
candle-light fell upon her wasted face and her gray
hair.
“ Do you know me, Jim ? ” she said.
Then there was a deep silence in the room, and
that Hand shook the blind again, and the wind
whispered, and the candle burnt lower.
“I’ve been a brute to you, Alice,” said the
man’s voice, more faintly than before. “But,
please Cod, we’ll be happy yet. How long have
I been here ?”
“ Nearly two weeks, Jim, and to-morrow is New
Year’s Day.”
“Then we’ll begin the year together, Alice.”
The voice was very faint now, and the candle had
burnt very low. “And we shall be rich. Fori
know where gold lies, in a gully in the hills. It’s
very dark, wife. But dark—or light—we’ll begin
the year—together.”
The wind brought a new sound now. It was
the ringing of bells, peal on peal, tolling for the
Old Year that was dead and rejoicing in the New
Year’s birth.
And in the room there was a whisper.
“Forgive me, Alice, and let us begin the
year .”
“ Oh ! Jim ! Jim !”
The candle flickered and went out. The wind
fell to a hush, and the sound of the bells came
softly from afar.
He had begun the Year.
B.
Dad’s Fault.
(FOR THE BULLETIN. )
“I drink; yes. But it’s Dad’s fault. Can you
blame me? Just listen a bit. What can a man
like me do?”
“What can others do? It isn’t every man’s
father is captain.”
“ That’s one way of looking at it, but the fact
that he’s captain is against me. Take the others
—every miner gets his pay and lives as he likes.
I don’t. I’ve to board with Dad. And he makes
his own charge, and the charge varies with the
number of days I work. He just cuts it to leave
me a trifle on pay-day. Is it any wonder I drink?
The heart is taken out of me.”
“ But the board and so on are good.”
“ Are they ? There are three hundred copper-
miners on the field, and any one of them can have
my boots. You see, boarding with Dad, I must
wash myself. He calls it behaving like a gentle-
man. See here, I must actually put on a boiled
shirt on Sundays. Do you wonder I drink ?
I’ve no objection to behave like a gentleman if
I’ve anything for it, but Dad won’t bleed. Do
you wonder I drink ?”
“An educated man
“ Educated ? There it is again ! Dad’s in it
on that side also. He gave me a bit of education,
but not enough to make anything out of. I know
enough to see these fellows’ ignorance, but what’s
the good of that? I don’t know enough to be
happy with myself. I count that a crime. I’d be
all right with the boys if I didn’t know so*much.
As an ignorant man I’d be as sober as-a judge ;
in fact, I’d likely be a temperance preacher ; and
as an educated man I’m sure I’d be sober. But
Dad dragged me out of ofte class and didn’t shove
me into the other. Thus I’m nowhere. Is it any
wonder I drink ?
“ Fine feelings ——”
“ Fine feeiings, nice manners—they’re”just like
the rest. Dad gave me scraps and shreds of these,
and where am I ? I’ve just enough to disgust me
with the fellows around. If I were rougher and
worse-mannered my stomach wouldn’t rise as it
does. If I were perfectly well - mannered I
wouldn’t notice or could successfully distract
mySelf. As it is, I’m in a hole. Do you wonder
I drink ? If I had the manners of a miner I’d be,
sober ; if I had the education of one I’d be sober ;
if I had the pay of one I’d be sober. But at eyery
point Dad sits on me. Do you wonder I drink?”
“ Well, marry and make a home of your own.”
“There it is. Dad’s there again. I had a
notion of Cissy. You know Cissy—long nose, big
byes, long hair, long fingers—just my style. Wfell,
what docs Dad do ? ”
“ But if you like the girl you needn’t mind
Dad.”
“ That’s all yon know. Why, Dad cut me out.
They’re engaged. Is it any wonder I drink ? ”
“ But you’ve been drunk now fully two years.”
“ And do yon wonder ? ”
CURRAGH.
The tape telegraph, in Jay Gould’s bed-
room, began ticking out the news of his death a
few minutes after it occurred, the instrument
working automatically with others. The incident
had been forecast in Bronson Howard’s play,
“ Henrietta. ”
The warship (of “Our Squadron ”) Mil-
dura’s boiler has been sold to the Newcastle
creamery. If the boiler was none too large for
the ship, and not in any Way defective, as the
officers declared, in contradiction to the men’s
assertion, why was a change necessary ?
AN EFFICIENT CURATIVE
For disorders of the stomach, liver, and bowels is
found in Wolfe’s Schnapps. It is effectual and
perfectly safe.

EDWARD DYSON
It is only fair to “J.L.P.,” who has
written much over the signature “Bakblox” in
many newspapers, to say that he is not the “Back
Blocks” who lately wrote a letter to The Bulletin
in favour of encouraging camels in the far West
Shortly after the completion of the tele-
graph line through a nigger-infested part of
Queensland a black'-boy was shot by one of the
station-masters just in time to prevent him
spearing the lineman. The S.M., being noV to
the homicidal portion of his duties, wired to head
quarters, as follows Accidentally shot black-
boy in station-yard—what shall Ido ?” About an
hour later the instrument ticked out: “ Bury him,
you infernal fool.”
The last duel fought between. British
officers was in July, 1853, between Captain Nolan,
ridingJnaster of the 15th King’s Hussars, and
•Captain Johnson, H.E.1.C.5., near Bangalore,
Madras. The duel was fought with swords, and
Captain Nolan killed his adversary. This Captain
Nolan was the same officer who carried the order to
Lord Cardigan to take the guns from the Russians
on‘Balaclava day. Nolan was shot dead in the
charge. It was of him that Lindsey Gordon
wrote; —
So he rode to death with that careless smile
In the van of the Light Brigade,
"So, stricken by Russian grape, the cheer
Rang out while he toppled hack
From his shattered lungs as merry and clear
As it did when he roused the pack.
Let never a tear his memory stain, give his ashes never a
sigh, > '
One of many who perished, and not in vain,
As a type of our chivalry.
Dear Bulletin, —You are dead against the
introduction of Kanakas'and other forms of cheap
labour. I wish you would start sugarcane-grow-
ing, and have to depend on the white labourers to
get your eane planted', weeded, and stripped.
You would "soon alter your views. You would
consign the whole of them to perdition before a
month passed. They—the white labourers—are a
cheeky, lazy,unthankful, worthless kit of wretches.
I am a workihg-raan ; have earned my own living
since I was 12 years of age. I hold a rented farm,
and grow sugarcane—about the only profitable crop
that can be grown at present in my part of the
country. I pay my men—for lam compelled. to em-
ploy—6s. a day, and provide them with firewood,
water, and sleeping accommodation. They work,
on an average, nine hours a day, and the work is not
heavy. It is nothing compared with navvying— }
speak from experience—fencing, or digging. I®,
child’s-play, or work, if you like. If the day is
hot, I allow the men to take from two to three
hours off in the middle of the day. Well, a
forlorn-looking individual, “ humping his blttey,
comes along, asks for a job, rolls off a fairy tale
about being ddad-beat, etc., etc., etc., and begsm
the name\)f God for employment. Perhaps lam
full-handed (I have six or eight men at work), but
out of pity I put him on. The. first week he will
work well,"the second week he flags considerably,
the third week he assumes a gp-io-blazes de-
meanour, and if I speak to him about his .
work, he throws down his hoe at once, lets -
|oose ’his flood of lingo at me, and winds-up
with a demand 1 for his sanguinary money. B®
gets it, and for a'few days he haunts the local,
pub., with a pair of black eyes and a patch of skm-
off his nose. Not that 1 mean to insinuate that L
or anyone at my suggestion, give him the said
coloured optics. I haven’t been at thiscane-groff-
ing business very long, and never had men in my
employ before, and am fast coming to the con-
clusion that I treat them too well, and speak top .
kindly to them. For the future I shall treat them
with very little consideration and no courtesy»
and when I have occasion to remind them tha
their hoes are not moving fast enough, I siia
intersperse my remarks with allusions to purga-
tory, blood, and other things dear to the heart 0 .
the working-man. And if half-a-dozen stray ,
Kanakas happen to wander along this way 1 sli»
hire a coach-and-four, and a brass band— u ■».
Bulletin— and go out to meet them. AO
although I shall continue to read The Bulleii.\
any articles dealing with the white working-ma
as against employers will be read in a scoffing) un-
believing spirit. I liavo sent you this on >
to relieve my pent-np feelings, for I tia ' ‘
been worried, harassed, annoyed and vexf-k.
more during the past busy planting season
than ever in my life before, and all by ,
protegtS the poor, down trodden shirking man.

QUEEN VICTORIA'S CHARITABLE CONTRIBUTION—AGAIN.
Queen Victoria has sent to Queensland her “sympathy” (unaccompanied by a cheque) and here you have a flooded-out family making use of it. The father is seen in
the foreground keeping himself and children dry with it, while in the middle distance may be seen the mother cooking some of it for their frugal meal, crooning in an
undertone “God save the Queen!”

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, ivith whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director,
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 toords, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
ivhen the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal -.- The Standard and Diggers'
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.
Notice to Subscribers In Victoria, Queens-
land, South Australia and Tasmania.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N. S. Wales in future will be £l, with Is. 2d.
added for postage. , ,
No papers will he sent unless the subscription be pre-
paid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY. FEBRUARY 18, 1893.
The Coming of Israel.
Last week theallegation, cabled from England,
that a shipment of some 400 destitute Jews
was en route for Australia, with the pros-
pect of an unlimited number to follow,
awakened this country to some disagree-
able possibilities of the future. The policy
of the United States, which has grown
weary of being the dust-bin of Europe, has
practically closed up the place of refuge to
which the superfluous humanity of the Old
World has mostly betaken itself for at
least half a century, and thereby has
come a sudden realisation of the extreme
smallness of the earth, and the increasing
lack of new countries to which immigration
can be directed. The globe looks large upon
the map, but the unoccupied regions are
very few, and those which are fitted for
European settlement still fewer. The latter
can be counted on the fingers of one hand—
South Africa, Australia, Maoriland, Argen-
tina, that is about all. Europe and Southern
Asia are already full of men and of misery.
The Canadian Dominion, Iceland, Siberia,
and Patagonia are all more or less frozen
and inhospitable, and the two latter are
practically impossible for political and other
reasons. From Mexico southward to Brazil
is mostly a sun-blasted region where there
is little wealth and many strange diseases,
and where the prospects of the settler are of
the poorest description. The same descrip-
tion applies to all Northern and Central
Africa. The South Sea archipelagos are
already overrun by that universal scavenger,
the Chinaman, Beyond the four countries
already named the world is practically closed,
and of these four Argentina is bankrupt and
lawless, and South Africa is swamped with
the curse of cheap native labour, so that, as
an opening for pauper immigrants, it is prac-
tically worthless. Of all the Promised Lands,
Australia and Maoriland are very nearly the
only ones that are left. And of the 600,000
or 700,000 pauper immigrants who used to
annually raid the United States, as many
as can pay their passages will probably one
day make for these provinces. It is more
than likely that nine-tenths of them will not
be able to pay their passages, and that half
the remainder will be deterred by the appar-
ently infinite distances to be traversed, but
even then the prospect is sufficiently awful.
• • *
r The proposed consignment of Jews may
yet prove the advance-guard of the great
army. If they are admitted there are more
and still more Jews to follow, and after
them more Jews yet, and where or when
the avalanche of Israel will cease if
once started —there is no telling. There
are, on a rough estimate, 6j millions of
Hebrews in existence ; of these, three
millions are located in Russia, and Russia
has given Israel notice to quit en masse.
One-half the tribes of Judah and Benjamin
are seeking a new location, and the country
that takes them in will become the greatest
Jewish power on earth. Australasia can have
half, or more than half, the Chosen People
for the asking, or without the asking. And
not only is the second exodus quite willing
to come this way, but along with the Chosen
we may have hordes of Poles, Hungarians,
Bohemians, and other illiterate paupers
who, being shut out of America, will pre-
sently join in the great movement south-
ward. The beginning of the pauper influx
is like the first small breach which the ocean
makes in an embankment; when once the
stream begins to flow it will never stop
until this country shuts its ports, and declares
that not one more outcast shall find refuge
in Australasia. Soon or late cheap labour
immigration must be absolutely prohibited,
and the sooner the better.
. * * *
/
The possible immigrants from Europe are
almost all objectionable, but of them all
the Hebrews are the most objectionable.
The Bulletin does not object to them
on account of their creed, for it has none
itself, and to it all faiths which do not
grossly outrage either sense or decency are
very much the same. Neither does it object
to their nationality ; to it all civilised white
races are practically alike. Moreover, it
fully recognises that, in many senses, the
Jewish race is the greatest the world has
ever seen, or is ever likely to see.' It has
more vitality than all the rest of the nations
combined. It has outlived all its conquerors,
and they were many ; it has seen the
Egyptian, Canaanite, Philistine, Phoenician,
Syrian, Assyrian, Babylonian, Persian,
Saracen, Scythian, Ethiopian, Parthian,
Greek, Roman, Norman, all die and go down
to the dust, and still it has lived on almost
unchanged, and practically unchangeable.
It promises to outlive the Teuton and the
Frank, and the even more enduring Spaniard,
and to buy and sell on the tomb of the last
of the Anglo-Saxons, and to see even the
Chinaman wither away into his native dust.
The Jew, the Pyramids, and the unspeakably
ancient city of Damascus are probably the
three oldest things in the Eastern hemi-
sphere, and, to all appearance, the Pyramids
will crumble into the voiceless desert, and
Damascus will wither by the shores of its
dried-up rivers before the sons of Abraham
vanish from among the nations. The Jew,
if he would come to Australia, and marry
and give in marriage, and sink his lonesome
identity among the people of this land,
might lay here the foundations of a nation
that would one day own the earth. He
is head and shoulders above all other
men in energy, brains, perseverance,
and everything that goes to make
success. In proportion to their numbers,
the Jews have done ten times as much for
science, art, and literature as any other tribe
or race in all history. It has been truly said
that there is to-day no German literature—
only a Hebrew literature in Germany, and
the same assertion is more or less true in
every land where the vulture beak of Israel
has ever loomed on the furthest horizon.
And with all this, and despite all this, the
Israelite is, next to the Chinaman, the most
undesirable immigrant who ever reached, or
is likely to reach, these shores.
• • •
For the Jew, wherever he goes, remains a
Jew for ever. Immigrants of all the other
nationalities of Europe may be absorbed into
the mass of the population, but this one in-
domitable Asiatic stands apart to all time.
No matter where he goes he is in a strange
land ; he has no land of his own, and is a
foreigner everywhere. He never, or hardly
ever, voluntarily fights for the country in
which he lives, no matter how extreme may
be its peril ; it is not his country and he has
practically no desire for its welfare, except
in so far as his own personal interests are
involved. Since the days when John of
Gischala and his Robbers defended the
crumbling walls of Jerusalem against Titus
and the Roman legions the Jew has practi-
cally abandoned the art of patriotism. He
never toils and, apparently, he never will.
He is essentially a trader, banker, usurer,
sweater, a being of commissions and per-
centages, a past-master in the mystery
of living on the labour of others. Also,
he is incidentally an artist, litterateur,
or politician. Wherever he goes wealth
somehow drifts slowly but surely into his
hands ; wherever he is left undisturbed the
population gradually divides itself into a
small Hebraic plutocracy and a vast multi-
tude of beggared Christians, who labour in
order that the Chosen People may pile up
more and still more riches. The date of his
emancipation is so very recent that the pro-
cess is nowhere complete, but in every
country where the Jew is placed on the same
political level as the rest of the population
things arc steadily moving towards that in-
evitable consummation. Wherever he goes
the aggregate wealth of the country increases,
but its diffusion becomes more and more
unequal, and the line of demarcation between
the rich and the poor becomes more and
more marked. In every land the Hebrew
is a commercial freebooter, a plunderer, an
atom in an army of occupation, one among a
flight of locusts who come to devour and
then, if necessary, to pass on. Nobody was
ever further removed from the ideal settler
in a new land than the typical son of Jacob
and of his brother Cohen.
* • •
It may seem, on the surface of things,
absurd to apply this description to the
illiterate, beggared, toil worn Hebrew of
Eastern Europe—those incarnations of ab-
jectness and misery who are even now
knocking at the door of civilisation and
humbly craving admittance. But the blood
of Abraham is in their veins, and there is
not one of the sons of that indomitable
patriarch who is not a potential danger. Ihe
Jews of Britain, France, Germany, Spain
and Italy were all, at one time or another,
nearly as hopeless, as wretched, and as
down-trodden as those of Russia are to-day,
but the instant the weight of political
oppression was removed the old Israelitish
spirit flamed out once more, and the people
who yesterday were slaves, to-day reach
forth their talons to grasp the earth and all
its contents. The reason why all Anglo-
Saxondom must close her gates against the
wholesale coming of Israel may be summed
up in a sentence —she dare not do otherwise.
This world is already suffering because workers
are too few and traders and speculators are
too many, and the very existence of a
nation which is nearly all traders and
speculators is a calamity. We may offer
a certain measure of barren sympathy to the
persecuted exiles from Russia, but more
than that we cannot afford to do. And even
that sympathy is somewhat tempered by the
knowledge that the brutal Tartar is only
retaliating, in his own unspeakably brutal
fashion, upon a people whose methods of
finance weighed upon him like a mountain
for many years. The 160 per cent. Hebrew
took enormous risks in his dealings with the
Russian, and the results have proved
disastrous —as might have been expected.
He who practises extortion upon a tiger is
always torn asunder by his debtor sooner or
later. y
Francis Adams to “The Bulletin” on
English Politics.
Dear Bulletin, —I have long intended to write
you a letter about some things in the last general
elections in England, but the opportunity only
conies now. Also, the necessity for telling you
the truth, or something more or less approach-
ing it, concerning the more visible phases of
English politics is not great.
* * #
The result of the elections can only be looked
upon in one way by any candid, unbiassed and
intelligent observer, and that is as a great Con-
servative victory. The English feeling in favour
of giving each party its turn is, perhaps, the
most potent factor in the whole system. After
the Tories have had a good innings, then let
the Liberals take the bat, and we will see what
the Tories can do in the way of bowling
and fielding, so reason many Englishmen.
Gladstone’s administration of 1868 lasted five
years, and was followed by Beaconsfield’s of
1874 for six, which was in turn followed by
Gladstone’s of 1880 for five years again.
Salisbury’s of 1886 lasted six years and
fourteen days. It committed blunders, many
and gross, one of them, the Parnell persecution,
peculiarly offensive to English sentiment. Yet,
with by far and away the most powerful living
political personality against them, tjie Tories
have been beaten by a bare majority of 40,
which has now fallen to 38. Several reasons
are assigned for this—the Irish expose over
Parnell’s deposition being the chief—but some
of the reasons which owjht to be assigned for it
are thrust into the background by the miserable
exigencies of party polemics.
# # •
The simple truth is that, take it all round,
this Tory Administration has been a really
good one in many ways. It is not only that it
has “run” the country, within and without,
on a direct line of policy which could be
honestly apprehended and justly judged, but it
has done more genuine democratic work than
any of the Liberal Governments which imme-
diately preceded it, Chamberlain and Dilkb
and the Radicals generally never, in the old
Liberal Administrations, had any real influence.
Chamberlain and his Liberal Unionists of the
Midlands have practically controlled the Tory
Government, and, instead of the Gladstonian
“ high-ialutin ” platitudes about Liberty, Jus-
tice, Humanity, Civilisation, Christianity, the
Almighty, and all the rest of it, a little actual
democratic work has been got through in more
than one province of the national life.
Gosohen’s conversion scheme is the biggest
thing that has been done in English finance
this century, and it is astonishing how little
credit he has got for it. As usual, it was one
of Gladstone’s perennial promissory baits, but
when he had the chance to carry the thing
through he funked, also, as usual; and now the
old rascal has the effrontery to deny and be-
little it! Had Chamberlain and Balfour had
their own way—had the last measures of the
Parliament been to entirely divorce local from
Imperial Governments —had England, Ireland,
Scotland and Wales been given at one
and the same moment a full and just con-
trol of their local affairs, then Gladstone
would have been permanently euchred at last,
or I am much mistaken, and the most stupen-
dous political fraud of our time, or any time,
have at last been definitely exploded.
# * #
Pray do not think I have become Toryfied.
This is not so. For, to me, both parties are
equally indifferent, equally hateful rather ,

The sterile Liberal tall talk is utterly sick

leads to nothing, ends in nothing. Of (u

does of Pakkes. But the Englishman is by f,I

weary of him that it drove him oft'. But
the country hadn’t found him out, and the
country forced him back on top of the political
world in 1880, just as it did Pakkes in Kew

have found him out since then, and know him
for the verbose and fiddling that
he is, but new sections arise to be
fooled by him still, and as I went away
from England a few months ago I was made
violently sick on land by a tremendous dose of
the old Liberty, Justice, Humanity, Civilisa-
tion, Christianity, and the Almighty stuff
before I was made more moderately sick on sea

PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Queensland Floods.
The last of many calamities has just descended
pou unhappy Queensland. No such flood has
occurred within the memory of local man as
ihat which has desolated Brisbane and the
adjacent towns. The damage is vaguely
estimated at £2,000,000 ; the loss of life is an
unknown quantity, and the misery involved is
beyond calculation. Brisbane to-day looks like
a fragment of the world the day after the
deluge; it is a steaming wilderness of fallen
bricks and slime and debris and unspeakable
rottenness. One half the Queensland navy and
various other vessels are stranded among the
trees in the Botanical Gardens. Another lost
vessel has been discovered inland among the bush.
Most of South Brisbane has altogether vanished.
The Victoria Bridge, valued at £140,000, has
mostly gone on a voyage down the river, and
its remains will probably be found at a future
date stranded somewhere in far-off Polynesia,
Xlie great railway-bridge has vanished into
nothingness. The houses of the poorer class of
the population—chiefly weatherboard structures
supported on four posts after the cheap and un-
gainly fashion of Australian architecture —have
been washed away bodily by hundreds. For
nearly two days a continuous procession of lost
homes was speeding down towards the sea,
and the cottages of innumerable starving
families are now tossing about in the
Pacific, How many of the inhabitants went
down the river along with the debris of
their fortunes is a mere matter of conjecture ;
probably, for years to come, the melancholy
“ Missing Friends ” column will be occasionally
haunted'with inquiries after people who were
ff last heard of in Brisbane about the beginning
of February, 1893,” Meanwhile, almost every
public structure in the city has been turned
into a refuge, where hundreds of outcasts—
whose goods and prospects are afloat on the
ocean are considering the evil days to
come, A month ago, Brisbane was the most
utterly bankrupt and poverty-stricken of all
the Australian capitals; now its condition
is akin to that of China in a year of famine.
The adjacent towns are almost equally desolate;
probably more than one-third of the population
of Queensland has been involved, more or less
directly, in the general disaster, and the
remaining two-thirds are too insolvent to lend
anything like efficient assistance. The occasion
is one when all Australia should come to the
rescue, and everything that Australia is likely
to do, in its present poverty-stricken condition,
will not be much more than a drop in the ocean
of calamity.

A Flea for tlie Republic.
Thus Walter Bagehot in the “English
Constitution
It is easy to imagine, upon a constitutional throne, an
active and meddling fool, who always acts when he should
not, who never acts when he should, who warns his
ministers against their judicious measures, who encourages
them in their injudicious measures. It is easy to
imagine that such a king should be the tool of others;
that favourites should guide him ; that mistresses
should corrupt him ; that the atmosphere of a bad
court should be used to degrade free government.
We have had an awful instance of the dangers of consti-
tutional royalty. We have had the cass of a meddling
maniac During the greater part of his life George lll.’s
reason was half upset by every crisis. Throughout his
life he had an obstinacy akin to that of insanity. He was
an obstinate and an evil influence ; he could not be turned
from what was inexpedient; by the aid of his station he
turned truer but weaker men from what was expedient.
He prolonged the American war,
perhaps he caused the war. So we inherit the
vestiges of an American hatred; he forebade
Mr. Pitt’s wise plans, so we inherit an Irish
difficulty. He would not let us do right iu time, so now
our attempts at right are out of time and fruitless. Con-
stitutional royalty under an active and half-insane king is
one of the worst of Governments. There is in it a secret
power which is always eager, which is generally obstinate,
which is often wrong, which rules Ministers more than
they know themselves, which overpowers them much
more than the public believe, which is irresponsible
because it is inscrutable, which cannot be prevented
because it cannot be seen. The benefits of a good
monarch are almost invaluable, but the evils of
a bad monarch are almost irreparable
A constitutional sovereign must in the common course of
government be a man of but common ability. I am
afraid, looking to the early acquired feebleness of
hereditary dynasties, that we must expect him to be a
man of interior ability. Theory and experience both
teach that the education of a prince can be but a poor
education, and that a royal family will generally have
less ability than other families. What right have we
then to expect the perpetual entail on any family of an
exquisite discretion, which, if it be not a sort of genius, is
at least as rare as genius? . . . The great abilities and
good intentions that are necessary to make an efficient and
good despot never were continuously combined in any
line of rulers. They are far out of the reach of hereditary
human nature. Can it be said that the characteristic
qualities of a constitutional monarch are more within its
reach ? I am afraid not. The characteristic use of
an hereditary constitutional monarch greatly surpasses
the ordinary competence of hereditary faculties.
, • ■. • ■ It is idle to expect an ordinary man
born m the purple to have greater genius than an
extraordinary man born out of the purple ; to expect a
man whose place has always been fixed to have a better
judgment than one who has lived by his judgment; to
expect a man whose career will be the same whether he is
discreet or whether he is indiscreet to have the nice dis-
cretion of one who has risen by his wisdom, who will fall
ii he ceases to be wise.

The Sacred Pocket of the Taxpayer.
There are some questions on which the Sydney
Morning Herald is hopelessly befogged;
others on which it attempts to befog its readers.
On the latter occasions its attempts are only
likely to succeed with those who are willing to
lie deceived. As a sample, take a late leader on
the unemployed difficulty, and the ideas of cer-
tain labour members that it might he solved by
means of village settlement. Even Parties—
in a sentence that almost paraphrased the
words of the labour member who first brought
village settlement before the Assembly’s notice
—has declared that “in a country where we
have untold acres of crude, unoccupied 'land, it
is a shame to have unoccupied human'creatures
without an effort to enable 'them to turn this
land into a fruitful region of the State tto which
they belong.” But the Herald— not objecting
(that money should be borrowed for the pur-
pose of constructing railways, whose chief pur-
pose was to enrich the land-grabber over whose
estates they ;ran— objects to money being
borrowed for the purposes of “relief works,”
as it disingenuously styles co-operativ'e settle-
ment, on the grounds that it (the money) must
■come out of the pockets of the taxpayer. Now,
we would ask the Herald to carry on its argu-
ment to its logical conclusion and advocate that
the unemployed should be allowed to starve. At
present the poor wh® can’t find remunerative
work are supplied “ out of the pockets of the
taxpayer,” publicly and privately with relief;;
hospitals, benevolent asylums and soup-kitchens
are constructed and maintained, also “ out of the
pockets of the taxpayer,” 'as are gads, for those
compulsorily made sick, feeble and criminal by
compulsory idleness and the evils that follow in
its train. In the interests of ** the pockets of the
taxpayer,” these persons should fee hurried out
of existence by being absolutely 'denied subsis-
tence ? But the Herald’s argument even more
disingenuously mis-states the case. It has
always been one of the main arguments of those
who favour village settlement, after stating
their desire to give men, willing to work, an
opportunity to be self-supporting, that it
would lighten the burdens of taxation. Those
to whom all the avenues of labour are closed
year after year (the numbers yearly increase
though the components may change) remain a
burden to the State; provided with fixed
employment by village settlement their first
charge, when the land became fully capable of
sustaining life, would be the repayment, by
instalments, of the State loan. That accom-
plished, the acres and the hands, once un-
occupied and a source of expense to the whole
community, might, under the leasehold system,
become a yearly source of revenue that would
amply repay the community for the initial
expense. Meantime, in the sacred name of
“the pockets of the taxpayers,” in the holy
interests of cash down, we ask the S.M. Herald
to be consistent and to call on the Government
to abolish at once all these charitable institu-
tions that never can by any means become
reproductive and self-supporting.

A Word to the N.S.W. Democratic Party.
Now that the N.S.W. Legislative Council has
passed the Electoral Bill with the addition of
the Argus vote for “ Thrift,” there is a very
plain and logical course open to the Hires
Ministry, That is to accept the Bill as it now
stands, and then resume the fight on the man-
hood suffrage principle next session. The
measure, even as botched by the House of
kidney-fits, contains many great improvements
on the present system ; among other things, it
reduces the bulk of the overgrown Assembly,
and it cuts down all the twelve and twenty-vote
properly-holders to two votes only, thereby
reducing the total voting power of mere bricks
and mortar very materially. If the Govern-
ment declines to accept it, the whole elaborate
scheme will have once more to be talked and
wrangled over next session from the first
clause to the last, and when another year has
been wasted, the Council may reject it al-
together, and the valuable advantages now offered
may be entirely lost. And, on the other hand,
if the Government accepts, at least three-fourths
of the great reform will be definitely secured.
Then, as the first public business of next session
(the House can be prorogued and another session
begun within ten days), it can introduce a bill
in one clause, providing that the last semblance
of the property-vote shall be abolished. Such
a bill could be passed through all its stages
in the Assembly (where no man dare oppose
it) in three hours, and if the Council thought
fit to reject it, there still remains the resource
of swamping that conservative institution with
new nominees. If the Ministry refuses to
accept the measure of success which it has
secured it can only be because it wants an
excuse for wrecking the manhood suffrage prin-
ciple indefinitely, and is glad of a chance to do
so and strike a noble, unyielding attitude at
one and the same moment; or else because it
wants the Electoral Bill as a war-cry with
which to stump the country in view of a coming
general election ; or—as a third alternative —
because it wants to spend next session in
wrangling over that weary, ill-fated bill once
more in order that it may not have time to
revise the tariff and attend to certain other
measures on which it might ultimately be
wrecked.
A correspondent sends to The Bulletin the
following cut from Walter Besant’s “ The
Captain’s Room,” written before the apostate
Griffith legalised the Queensland South Sea
slave-trade :
A man may carry Chinese coolies to Trinidad, Peru, or
Cuba, but under what pretences he inveigles them aboard 1
what promises he makes them, and how much he gets for
each, no one outside the trade, which is a limited com-
pany, knows or discovers. You might sooner hope to
learn the secret of the Roval Arch. Again, you
may ship cooliis for Reunion. They are British
subjects, but they are taken on board at
Pondicherry, which is a French settlement. And
the like mystery surrounds each transaction in Hindoo
flesh. Lastly, there is a delightful pastime still carried
on in Polynesia, known as 11 the labour traffic.” Opinions
differ as to the beneficial results of a few years of coolie-
dom in Queensland. For, whereas some authorities say
that the Polynesian learns the blessings of second-hand
reach-me-down-suits with a smattering of Christianity,
with which to astonish his relatives, the Browns, on ids
return, others declare that the extra garments are dis-
carded as soon as he lands, the rudiments of the
Christian faith forgotten, and only the taste for ruin
remains. I know not which is right, because, in
order to decide the point, one ought to live along
with the native Polynesians and with Australian
colonists, in order to hear both sides of the question, and
no controversialist has as yet done that. One thing, how-
ever, is quite certain, that the coolies embark for various
reasons, among which no one has as yet pretended to find
a desire to toil on the Queensland sugar plantations. Toil
of any kind is indeed the last thing which these children
of the Equatorial Pacific desire. Rest is what they love,
or, if any exercise, then a languid swim in tepid waters,
a dance.
Hear Bulletin, —The greatest troubles in
Australian politics seem to lie that every
Government promises to have a surplus at the
beginning of the year and pans out with a
deficiency ; and every Cabinet starts the session
by promising to pass Urgent measures and never
does pass 'them ; and every member promises
his constituents that 'he will dh his utmost to
support certain principles, and then goes back
on them. Nebody—'until now—has found any
remedy for this accursed inconsistency, but it
is unanimously agreed that a remedy is urgently
wanted. Therefore, I wish to offer the follow-
ing suggestion, which, ; I think, not only pro-
vides a positive cure for almost every kind of
political inconsistency, but is also calculated 'to
appeal Strongly 'to the instincts of a sporting
community. Let'every politician be required
to back his consistency with a bet. Fdr in-
stance, the Treasurer must, in the first place,
be required to bring out a visible cash surplus
every year-—when he doesn’t bring out a sur-
plus ihis billet 'thereby becomes vacant. When
he produces his‘estimates and states the amount
of the prospective surplus he should be'required
to lay the Consolidated Revenue £lOOO to £5O,
which, I think, are reasonable'odds,that the said
surplus will be forthcoming. He must depend
to some extent, of course, on his subordinates,
but they should also be required to bet on the
accuracy of their departmental estimates, which
would probably keep them straight. When
Government promises to pass 'certain measures
■during the session,, it should be compelled
to back itself separately on each item, and
if it fails to pass any measure, it must
pay tip. A special official, to be called the
State Bookmaker, could collect the bets and
keep the accounts. In a similar fashion, the
candidate who promises to vote for certain
principles should be made to back Ihis con-
sistency with his constituents at reasonable
odds, and put the money up with the nearest
reliable bank; all lost bets to go to the
local School of Arts. In this manner, a con-
sistent and energetic Ministry could make con-
siderable additions to its salary, and a Cabinet
which was a chronic failure would presently be
sold up by the State Bookmaker and abolished.
Ministers who hadn’t the ready cash to put up,
could give orders on their salary. The Minis-
terial bets would, of course, apply to the
Assembly only ; the Government represen-
tative in the Council would have to back
himself separately. In consideration of
the extra difficulty of his position, he would
necessarily give much shorter odds than
the Ministers in the Lower House —in
fact, the Consolidated Revenue should lay
even money to the representative of a Radical
Cabinet in the average Council. Any member
moving a no-confidence resolution should be
required, as a preliminary, to back himself to
win for, say, £lOO at sto 1. Also a “ tote ”
should be open in the House every night, and
every member desiring to move an adjourn-
ment, or divide the House, or make any similar
interruption should first of all have to take a
£5 ticket, the member whose motion is carried
to scoop the pool, less 10 per cent, which might
go towards reducing the State debt. This pro-
position, I hold, is strictly constitutional.
When a man wants to stand for Parliament the
Treasury compels him to gamble £4O to nothing
that he will get a certain number of votes —in
other words, be is required to bet that he will
get in or somewhere near it. Why shouldn’t
he also be required to bet that he’ll keep
reasonably straight when he is in ?—Yours, &c.,
Titus Salt,
Dear Bulletin, —In the last will and testa-
ment of the late Charles Dickens are the
following emphatic words : —“ I direct that my
name be inscribed in plain English letters on
my tomb, without the addition of ‘Mr.’ or
‘ Esquire.’ I conjure my friends on no account
to make me the subject of any monument,
memorial, or testimonial whatever. I rest my
claim to the remembrance of my country on
my published works, and to the remembrance
of my friends upon their experience of me in
addition thereto.” The meaning of these words
cannot be mistaken. They were set down a
year before the novelist’s death, whilst his
faculties were undimmed. This desire of the
great novelist has been scrupulously regarded
by his countrymen in Britain. In Westminster
Abbey a plain slab in the floor denotes his last
resting-place, and his name is plainly carved on
the slab. There is no monument, bust, or
statue, such as invariably mark the resting-
places of England’s other illustrious dead. A
proposal to erect a statue of Dickens was
recently defeated in London County Council, on
the ground that it would be a violation of his
last plainly-expressed desire. It remained for
Parkes to flout the injunctions of Dickens.
That poet’s eye, in a fine frenzy rolling,
lighted upon Sydney Centennial Park, and
the gifted statesman and poet proceeded to
set up there statues to other gifted statesmen
and poets ; and now the landscape is spoilt by a
series of frock-coated, trousered, vulgar, marble
presentments of Disraeli, Gladstone, Lin-
coln, Thackeray, Dickens, and other victims.
To call these works of art would be absurd.
They are worthy neither of the men they
represent nor of the Australian nation. They
are worthy only of Mr. Henry Parkes. And
especially the stumpy, stodgy statue of the
dead novelist, Dickens, whose last sacred in-
structions are thereby grossly violated, is a
double insult to the dead and the living—a
disgrace to the Government of New South
Wales which sanctioned its erection, and a dis-
grace to any Government which allows it to
remain standing after public attention has been
called to it.—Yours truly, Savage.

French journalist, hints the possibility of a
Franco-German alliance, as “the struggle of the
Governments of Europe against the rising wave of
Socialism is far more important than any terri-
torial question.”

A new and somewhat stragglesome com-
pany opened at Sydney Royal on Saturday night
in “The World Against Her,” a big, solid drama
in six massive slabs, with great quantities of dis-
tressed heroine in it, a large, healthy villain,
sundry unshaven under-villains, a stolen che-ild
ill-used in a damp cellar, a funny man, and a raw
hero. The drama is based on the well-known fact
that when a devoted and happy wife calls at her
husband’s place of business at a time when he
happens to be out, that husband’s affection
immediately fades away, ho becomes eaten up by
dark suspicions, and presently gets a divorce.
With this well-known and old-established circum-
stance for a' basis, the piece prospers remarkably.
The divorced wife is driven back and forward
over the face of England until the soles of
her feet get hard with much travelling. Ou four
separate occasions she haughtily orders the villain
to leave the house, with her arm stretched out in
front of her and tho hand pointing slightly down-
wards ; on one occasion he laughs a bitter laugh
and goes, and the other three times he smiles
triumphantly and doesn’t go. The funny man
once engages in mortal combat with the principal
hired ruffian, and knocks him out. The hero three
times walks rapidly out of the room in a broken
voice, driving the door violently in front of him,
and once he stands in an unnatural calm amid the
ruins of his lost happiness. On eight separate
occasions the suffering mother clings fondly to her
only daughter; and twice the
Inst girl laughs the reckless laugh
of despair and refuses to quit
her life of gilded sin. The
troubles of the wronged wife
end in an ivy-c'ad cottage with
creepers over the front porch,
and the villain wears evening
dress most of the time.
# * #
The company consists
largely of tail. Mr. Baker is a
light and smiling villain who
seems supremely happy in his
wickedness, and he can be highly
recommended to any family
which wants a really agreeable
scoundrel about the house. The
lost girl is Miss Edith Blande,
who plays much better than
she used to do, but who looks
so dreadfully and utterly lost
that her reformation is practi-
cally hopeless. Mrs. Molyneux
is evidently unhappy in the
uncongenial character of the
damaged wife, though she does
good work in the traditional
black dress; but that joyous
comedian, Harry Leston, Miss
Flora Anstead, and Mr. Plim-
mer (as a brutal ruffian in a
cellar) are all commendable.
The rest of the company con-
sists mainly of a feeble and
sketchy young lady, a dreadful
person with uncertain legs, a
banged and ill-used woman in
a damp place underground, a
wobblesotne individual with a
dreary countenance, like the
countenance of a horse, and
another man. The hero is
p'aved by the other man, whose
distressing woo dullness was
never so apparent before, and
who grits his teeth dreadfully in
moments of emotion, and seemingly tries to hois
tuT Hell- the „' V T Bt - , ? ome , day or ol ! ,er
The Bulletin will be betrayed into saying
something severe about the other man.

“The Times,” afc Sydney Criterion, are
not out of joint, and that tragic comedy is doing
about as much business as the house will hold. It
looked exceedingly well the first night, and it has
looked even better since. There is a powerful
moral lesson in every one of the characters, from
the elevated draper and his cub down to old
woman Hooley. Titheradge’s faint and shambling
old fop grows more wonderful day by day; and
the problem of how such a person could continue
to live, and wobble on his stiff legs over the face
of nature, and daily bounce the Scythe-bearer
with the hollow pretence that ho was still too
young to die, is past explanation. The awful
drunkenness and ponderous stupidity of the cub,
and the fatuousness of young woman Hooley— old
woman Hooley’s daughter—and the derangement
of the broken and shattered draper, have lost none
of their interest. In fact, “ The Times” is as good
as new and promises to continue indefinitely.
*« * *
A benefit performance—the biggest, it is
hoped, ever given in Sydney—will eventuate at
Her Majesty’s next Wednesday (22nd) in aid of
the sufferers by the Queensland Hoods. Among
those who will assist are the Criterion, Opera
House, Her Majesty’s, and Gaiety Burlesque
companies, Messrs. Mayne, Goodman, McDonald,
Bailey and Spong, and most of the other members
of the profession now in Sydney. Mr. Rignold
gives his theatre for the occasion, and Messrs.
Rolf and Marcus and Andrew undertake to do
the printing free. Every local individual of im-
portance, and many individuals who want to look
important, will be present or will lend their
“patronage.’’ Messrs. Titheradge, Courtneidge
and Phillips arc the committee in charge; Mr,
Bailey acts as secretary, and Mr. G. L. Goodman
as treasurer.

Sydney Garrick is next Saturday to be re-
christened the “Tivoli,” and Harry Rickards
establishes himself there as a permanent institu-
tion. The Bulletin wishes him every success ;
and at the same time it begs to state that it regards
the new name as stupid, meaningless, and suffi-
ciently hackneyed to bring ill-luck on any theatre.
FALK, PHOTOGRAPHER.
MR. G. L. GOODMAN (Treasurer, Sydney Lyceum Theatre).
Mr. Geo. L. Goodman, whose portrait
Appears in this week’s issue, is probably about the
best known man in N.S.W. theatrical circles. An
Englishman by birth, and a Hebrew by religion,
he was born in the Commercial-road, London, in
1 845 . and first came to Sydney in ’54. He finally
sett l e d in Australia in ’GO. He appeared with
success as an amateur actor, in 70, at the
Frmce of Wales Theatre (now known as Sydney
Royal), and for nine years afterwards was recog-
pised as the leading amateur of the city. In ISJ9
“ e made his first theatrical venture, when he
tourcH the country with Camilla Urso, the famous
violinist. In June 1880 he became acting-manager
and treasurer at Sydney Royal, then under the
management of Mr. Arthur Garner, and con-
tinned in that position until the old structure
was burnt in ’O2. During that period he
represented Messrs. Williamson, Garner and
Musgrove (individually and collectively), Allison,
Lazar, Majeroni, Hiscocks, Dampier, Bland Holt,
Simonsen, Brough, Boucicault, Wybert Reeve
and loole. He has received valuable presenta-
t'ons from almost all the leading actors and
actresses wdio have visited Australia of recent
m’. jndiiig Dion Boucicault (senior), Fred,
Lt-sli' l , S ira Bernhardt, Nellie Farrell,
Bei-re, Mrs. I’ojtcr, ami several dozen others, also
rc.-ently from the citizens of Sydney, and (in
Rom the Hebrew Philanthropic Society, of
which he acted for 11 years as secretary. His
marriage with a charming young Jewish lady is
anticipated at an early date. Mr. Goodman is
now noting-manager and treasurer at Sydney
Lyceum.
** * *
On Saturday last a swimming carnival was
held at Manly for the benefit of Mr. E. Cavill, the
well-known swimmer. There were 12 events on
the programme, including a children’s swimming-
match, diving exhibition, all-clothes race, polo
match under water, and the famous rescue of the
drowning man. The drowning man, Mr. R. A.
Luckham, of Thk Bulletin, fell overboard in his
bell-topper and jewellery, and being captured by
Mr. Cavill was towed ashore in haste by the hair,
his rescuer pouring water over him from the
top-hat as they went along to revive him.
The amateur championship was won in fine style
by Mr. W. G. Anslow, The “ all-clothes race,”
for swimmers in full dress, fell to Mr. F. Smith, a
person burdened with a bell-topper being left far
in the rear. The net profits amounted to <£3s.
Dr. Molyneux acted as referee, and gave every
satisfaction.
* * tic- *
The doors of Melbourne Royal didn’t close
with the pantomime season after all. On Satur-
day night the public were invited to roll up and
whoop in a patriotic manner for a company
described as “an Australian Natives’ Association,
consisting of descendants from old colonists, who
formauy years were popular, talented and well-
known members of the dramatic and musical pro-
fession.” For the sake of their alleged ancestors
The Bulletin withholds its everlasting curse
from the principals of this weird, peculiar com-
pany, without acknowledging the claim of any
young person to trade upon the fact that his or
her grandfather was in the same line of business.
The Natives’ Association now under notice would
seem to have started off with an idea of being a
juvenile opera crowd, and to have slightly departed
from its first resolution in consequence of chil-
dren being scarce. As compared with Tom Pol-
lard’s chickens these are old birds, innocent of
stage art, but no longer remarkable for joyous
infancy. Generally speaking, they are neither
young enough to interest, nor sufficiently
seasoned to be worth consideration in
strong acting opera like “ Rip Van Winkle.” A
lanky, treble-voiced stripling, oppressed by the
necessity for seeming a reckless inebiiate or a
pathetic ruin, is not much of a dramatic spectacle,
especially when he hasn’t a full allowance of face
or nose. And “Rip Van Winkle,” either as an
opera or drama, is entirely dependant on the per-
formance of the name part. Seme of the young
girls engaged are promising vocalists, noticeably
Minnie Sexton, Evelyn Harte, and Cynthia
Cary, but taken seriously, the show is rather a
regretable incident, the chorus and orchestra being
as thin as the ankles are thick. The best points
about “ Rip ” are probably the scenery and danc-
ing, in which latter department a Miss Ada
Lempriere showed a good deal of agility and
grace. The attraction of a little free and easy
kicking will scarcely establish this Natives’ Asso-
ciation on a paying basis, however.

Next Saturday the Royal Comic Opera
Company proper will come back like the prodigal
to Melbourne Princess’, bringing their fatted
calves with them. It is so long a time since
beautiful Ryley and the rest went away that they
will open out quite fresh on this occasion, and are
sure of a warm welcome from a city which has
had to subsist upon two pantomimes and a circus
for weeks at a stretch. “Dorothy” is appointed
to lead the way through a series of short revivals,
and the appearance of a new Lurcher in George
Lauri promises a spice of excitement in the hear-
ing of this oft-told tale,
** » *
Meanwhile ‘‘The Forty Thieves ” is ebbing
away in a succession of complimentary benefits.
The Faust Family farewelled on Tuesday, and
Lonnen enjoyed an extra special matinee the fol-
lowing afternoon. To-morrow (Friday) the last
night of the season, will be dedicated to Snazelle,
who, at time of writing, was making extensive
preparations for a grand programme. Not that
there is an instance on record of any programme
being other than “grand.”
** * *
The Circus thrives as before, in spite of
the unlooked-for amiability of the four lions and
their distant acquaintance, the Bengal tiger. One
day last week a violent hot wind blew holes in
the tent and necessitated a cessation of business,
but with this trifling exception, Fillis has been a
constant boom. The Water Pantomime appeals
considerably to householders whose supply of
temperance liquor is limited these times, and
although this novel item is decidedly weak in
aquatic low comedy, the fault might be easily
remedied by emptying a barrel of whisky into the
stage, and allowing the elephants to dive under
the pantomime until they became drunk and dis-
orderly. In their present condition, the elephants
are somewhat tiring. Also the educated pig
would be all the better for getting beastly intoxi-
cated.
** * *
To-day (Thursday) the National Baby
Show commences operations at the Exhibition.
Two or three hundred pledges of affection are
coloured on the card, and it is rumoured that the
sale price of each cherub will lie marked in plain
figures. The Gaiety and St. George’s Halls
struggle along bravely, but the usual Saturday
night concert at the Exhibition will this week be
replaced by the groans of disappointed parents
who haven’t scooped a prize.
** * *
On Friday the Circus gives a matinee for
the relief of the Queensland flood sufferers. Mr.
Fillis will hand over the gross receipts, so people
paying their money at the pigeon-holes can con-
sider t hat they are making a contribution to the
Flood Fund, and getting a free-pass to the show.
•T. C. Williamson, also, is already to the fore in
this good cause, and a meeting of the purfesh to
organise a gigantic day performance at the Prin-
cess' was held last Monday. For the outcome of
which meeting see advts.

Mrs. Langtry, writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, has offered a reward of £5O for the discovery of
the person or persons who originated and circulated the
report that she had been ejected from the Savoy
restaurant and requested to take her patronage else-
where. A very pretty piece of bluff. The story was that
she was dining or supping at the Savoy with young Peel
and another man, and became so obstreperous that she
was requested to leave the place. She says she did not
give a supper on that day, and therefore the whole tale
must be a fabrication. This is said to be what did
happen. The Lily was supping with Reel and another
man or two, and in course of the evening Peel
made some observation which eiidenlly gave of-
fence, for Lily said, “If you say that again, Bobby,
1 will throw this in your face holding up a glass of
champagne. “Bobby” repeated (he remark, and forth-
with she let fly the glass, which he, by deftly ducking his
head, escaped, but the wine went over the dress of a lady
who was sitting at the.next table. There was a rumpus,
naturally, hut apologies and explanations followed and
were accepted, and the incident was closed, as (hey say in
diplomacy. It was a pure accident, though somewhat an
unpleasant one for the lady whose dress was spoilt.
The English now do not take their pleasures sadly.
Nothing suits present taste, says The Bulletin’s corres-
pondent, that has no fun in it. With the exception of
three houses, all the London theatres are now running
either farcical comedy or comic opera, and it looks as if it
paid, for good audiences are the rule. At the Opera
C( miquo they have put up “ Trooper Ohirette,” described
as a musical-farcical-comedy, though it has few, if any, of
those elements about if. Willie Edouin and Fred.
Mervyn are in the caste, and Alice Atherton is leading
lady. The Shaftesbury will shortly open with “La
Rosihre,” said to be an adaptation from the French by
Harry Monkbouse. Frank Thornton has a part in this,
hut is sadly put out because be lias no song. He plays
instead of Monkbouse himself who cannot get a release
from bis engagement with Horace Sedger. From what I
hear, the piece is not likely to niter the fortunes of the
unfortunate Shaftesbury.

# * •
We have received a lob of interesting informa-
tion about the position and prospects of the Maria Island
Co recently floated in England for £250,000. The British
shareholder having parted with his good red gold at the
instance of the smart Melbourne men, it would be cruel
to say anything which would cause him uneasiness. As
thing's stand, the omnivorous British capitalist has got
Maria, who is now pretty ancient, and has seen many
vicissitudes, and some more British coin seems likely to
take root out here for the benefit of Australia. We have a
strong impression that the place is a very ordinary island,
and has little to recommend it barring the ozone and the
scenery.
* * »
It is tolerably certain that the Melbourne
Tramway Co., which snorted at the public when the
dividend was 72 per cent., will soon be starved into reduc-
ing its rates. Whereas short-distance railway travelling
is still cheaper in Melbourne than in London, the local
tramway fares are 33 per cent, higher on an average, some
of the cockney’s twopenny rides being longer than any
you can get in Marvellous M. for threepence. If the
trams are to hold their own with the trains the private
00. must reduce fares or else the national company must
raise them.
• * *
For a considerable time past it has rained in
Sydney every Sunday, and been reasonably fine all the
rest of the week. This is believed to be Heaven’s only way
of getting level with the hull-headed Port Jackson S.S.
Co., which has just raised the return fare to Manly by 50
per cent., and which in no small degree depends on its
Sabbath excursion trade.
# # •
The Anglo - Australian Investment, Finance
and Land Co. of Sydney—it is generally the company w ith
the longest name that makes the most lamentable collapse
—reports upon its prospects, or rather, on the empty
place where its prospects used to be. When it closed up
a very short time ago it was understood (hit it had
abundant assets and was entirely solvent, only it was
I difficult, to “ realise.” Now it appears that there is about
£3BS,(LG nominally available to be called up, and even
when this is realised, so far as it can be, there will not be
“ nearly enough ” to pay the depositors and debenture
holders. Which, to the unofficial eye, reads as if the
Anglo-Australian &c., &c. Co., in.its “solvent ” days, had
lost all its paid-up capital, and over £300,000 of the
deposits as well. Anyhow, there is such a black and
horribh discrepancy between the liquidators’ reports and
the balance-sheet that a whole lot of independent investi-
gation appears to be wanted.

Can anyone remember when there was last a buyer
of Queensland Nat ional Bank shares quoted on the Sydney
market? The Bulletin can’t. The eternal quotation,
“seller, £5,” “seller, 975. 6d.,” “seller, 945. 6d.,” and so
on means practically nothing ; there may he no buyer
above 995. Cd, or half-a-sovereign, orhalf-a-crown, or three-
pence, or £4 13s. 9d., or anything. In Federal Bank
shares the same eternal seller went right down the scale
from about 40s. to 7s. Cd., and all that time no buyer
turned up.

* • •
The attention of the N.S.W. Minister of Justice
has been directed to the affairs of that extraordinary
institution, the Trans-Pacific Insurance Co. of Sydney, now
in liquidation. It is indeed marvellous that so flagrant a
case of commercial crookedness should for so long have
escaped the eye of the law.
* * •
The writer fears that a N.S.W. Miningon Private
Property Act, though admittedly needed, will not do one-
twentieth the good the enthusiasts predict. There are
few places, where payable gold is known to exist, that are
closed to diggers. The rush to two recently-opened
estates (one at Rockley, the other also in the West) has
been shortlived, because they soon proved failures. Yet
these were described as rich so long as they were closed.
Others will be opened in a few months, only to be closed
again. Already all land, alienated in any gold-district
since ’Bl, is open to mining under the present system of
free-and-easy title. An Act is required to deal with
estates previously alienated.

The difference between the prices obtained for
Vic. Treasury bonds by the present and late Governments
respectively, is too slight to warrant the “ blow ” made
about it.

One of the directors of a Victorian mine,
recently floated in London, was credited on the prospectus
with the affix 11. A. From this gentleman’s airy disregard
of all rules of orthography and syntax nobody had thought
he possessed a University education, and enquiries
elicited the fact that the letters signified “ Mining
Agent.”
# # #
A backblock selector lately came to Sydney to
get a loan on his property. He went to one of the finance
companies, and while waiting to see the manager heard
the “click, click ” of a type-writer in an adjoining room.
“ What’s that noise? ” he asked a man who sat near him.
“ That’s the mortgages bucking about in the safe while
the interest is accumulating on ’em,” was the reply.
“ Jee-rusaiem ! ” exclaimed the backhlocker reaching
for his hat.
# * *
He came, saw, and left the countfy disappointed,
if not disgusted—Who? Mr. T. A. Bennett, perhaps the
strongest mining man who ever left England for
Australia. He was rich himself and represented
millions ; he arrived, fortunately 7 for himself, unheralded;
as far as was possible he travelled alone not only over Queens-
land, but also over nearly every N.S.W. mining field,
nevertheless he was besieged with men who had mines for
sale. Those lie would have liked to buy were not for sale,
and those for sale he did not consider worth purchasing.
On the principle that the first loss was the best, he spent
big sums of money in prospecting undeveloped propenies
only to meet with disappointment in one form or another.
His aim was, of course, to purchase a going concern which
would give a greatly-increased output with a proportion-
ate reduction of cost. He declared that a big body of
lOdwt. rock would satisfy ipm, and it says very little
either for the country or its judgment that he was unable
to find ore in N.S.W. Of course, owners condemned his
judgment after he condemned their mines, but those who
gauged him as a fool with money never made a sadder
mistake. He did not want the lOdwts. in impalpahly
fine gold or sulphurets, such as would require chlorination
or cyanide to treat; he did not want it in a narrow shoot,
but in a big reef that would average so much in free gold
throughout. If found to his satisfaction, money would
not have slopped him. If such mines exist ami are for
sale, the writer confesses that he knows not their where-
abouts, though he expects now to hear of many who
“ have just the thing.” For the True Blue Block mine,
Croydon, he made an offer of £20,000 cash, which was
declined. He was prepared to purchase the Mitchell’s
Creek mine, near Wellington (N.S.W.), hut not at the
price asked. The result of his visit will not help to boom
Australian mines in London.

Editor Bulletin, —Your par. of Jan. 14 rc
revolving stamp is, I think, not quite correct. The im-
provement to stamps was patented in Melbourne, in ’Ol,
by a man now living here, and was worked by him at
Maldon (Vic.) for several years in the Eaglehavvk Associ-
ation’s claim. He received £5O from the firm of F. N.
Russell & Co. (Sydney) for the right to use it; afterwards
lie sold it to Webster Bros, for £3OO. He thinks they took
it to Maori land in ’6S or ’Of). That was all he ever received
for ids simple but clever invention. He has now fallen on
evil days, his eyesight is very bad, and, although a good
mechanical engineer, is almost destitute through inability
to obtain employment. Even one pound of the million
lie foolishly missed would be very acceptable at present.
H's only hope now is an improvement in steam injectors
for which lam trying toobtain a patent. He has.nothing,
and i haven’t much. I could manage the thing in N.S.
Walls or Victoria, but I have been advised that it is
worth patenting in Great Britain and the U.S.—Yours,
W.F., Blayney, N.S.W.
* * *
The Duchess Tribute, Bendigo, has crushed 15
tons for 1700z5., most of which came from less than a
single ton. There is not much probability of the rich
gold lasting long, as any shot may show that it has gone.
It would he refreshing to hear one of the many rich
N.S.W. patches so described. The moment gold is struck
in most N.S.W. mines the manager wires to Sydney the
discovery, with an estimate of what it will go per ton, and
in many oases fails to get a ton to crush.
# # #
The Franklin Harbour Co., a Sydney-owned
mine in S.A., has held its meeting. Usual story: no
funds available ; obliged to suspend work, but no effort
was being spared to induce investment of sufficient
capital.
• * #
The worst-paid mining-managers in the world
are probably those of Bendigo, with the result that the
best of them are constantly leaving, and many others
would have gone long ago if unanchored by family ties.
Five pounds weekly is considered a big wage there ; many
a man has left £4 a week on Bendigo for a £lO berth else-
