846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
15th February, 1893.
My Dear Moorabinda—
Lady Jersey having consented to act as Presi-
dent of the Australasian Home Reading Union
until the end of the current year, that rickety in-
stitution will probably hold out for another 18
months. But ’twill then utterly fade away, for the
thought that 500 or more Unionists are fagging
through the same tome at a given time will fail to
sustain the interest in a grass-widowed concern
with its president 10,000 miles away.
“O for the touch of a titled hand!” How
many trusting societies have had their esprit dc
corps taken away by an enhandled lady among her
luggage? Lady Jersey hasn’t done the right
thing by the Bowdlerised Home Readers in under-
taking to preside in spirit so long after her depar-
ture. For if (such things have happened) the new
Governor’s spouse is touchy about being a mere
vice-president, the fact of Lady J. hanging on to
the post simply signs the Society’s death-warrant.
Without the light of Lady Battersea’s (oris it to be
Lady Brassey’s?) countenance the Union will die
the death.
Judged from the standpoint of Caringtonian
history, Gov. Jersey’s following of one Earl, a
Captain, an Honorable and a plain Mister
(you can’t count young Villiers, who is
fenced in, as it were), haven’t been very ardently
attracted by Australian beauty. Only one en-
gagement amongst four, unless you believe all the
girls who were engaged, or as good as engaged, to
the unfortunate Lord Ancrum —after he was dead.
Perhaps if Gov. Jersey had stuck to office as long
as the Baron did, Australian belledom would have
stood a better chance, but, as it is, the Jerseys’
term hasn’t done much for local spinsterhood.
The Carington regime was responsible for the
settlement of three local belles—yes, three members
of his suite found bliss in the beastly kawlinies,
and ii fourth tried very hard to settle down, only
the rich woman he haunted wouldn’t take him
(altho’ the ring was bought), and the other girl’s
dowry wasn’t up to expectations. By the side of
these connubial sparks, the small Jerseyan social
score of one engagement looks very little indeed.
Let us hope it is the brief visit that is most to
blame.
The Jersey children are being packed ’Ome by
the Ophir, and Gov. and Lady Jersey propose
leaving by the Catterthun early in March. The
vice-regalities evidently don’t intend to prolong
the sweet sorrow of parting, and the various com-
mittees for harassing the poor Earl, who, accord-
ing to London Times, is simply weary of the
incessant ceremonials, have to rush about at a
tremendous rate, and all talk at once so as to get
matters arranged in time. Of course there is to
be a big Town Hall dinner, and a huge illuminated
address is to be given the Earl as a reference from
his last place. I wonder how many emus they’ll
put in it ?
I don’t know why “The World Against Her,”
at Sydney Royal, should be described as the
ladies’ play. The one thing needful in a ladies’
play is a wardrobe, and the leading lady in “The
World,” &c., is sadly persecuted, and shows it in
her garments--which is as good as saying that she
begins in an innocent pink frock; gets on to
mousey grey; sinks deeply into black; and
emerges in the final scene in a half-and-half smile-
and-sob sort of garment that looks anything but
frisky, so that the hero may turn up his eyes and
say, rapturously, “She has not been led astray !
That frock is too unbecoming !”
Mrs. Molyneux (bouqueted after the second act)
as Madge Carlton looked best in the babyish
frock of pink worn in the first scenes—but why
did she top it off with so wicked-looking a Paris
hat? A cluster of extremely saucy roses dancing
on their stems seemed to augur that the villain
might have a chance after all if there was a
spring-bonnet-shop in the neighbourhood. Edith
Bland, in the white-silk ball-robes of stage-
iniquity, accentuated the fact that heelless white
satin shoes may be easy, but they seem to crowd
the stage too much when a tall lady’s “daisy
roots ” are in natural proportion to her height;
also, I must object to the way Miss B. treated her
feather boa—she whisked it about as if she was in
the habit of skipping with it.
How very like all one’s preconceived notions of
the Prince of Wales ! “Do you notice any change
in the physique of the women in England,” asked
he of Premier Dibbs. Mr. Dibbs did, now that his
attention had been called to it. He found them
finer and larger than they used to be. “Yes,”
remarked the heir-apparent, “we have been
wondering greatly about it during the last 10 or 12
years.” When the nation observes Albert Edward
lost in profound meditation it isn’t the State that
is absorbing him. He is worrying about the
physique of his lady subjects—he lias been wonder-
ing about the breadth of Miss Johanna Bull’s
shoulders and the lovely fall of her back for the
last 12 years without being able to make them out.
Amybody would see in two winks that theathletio
girl must develope into a stronger creature than
her vapourish grandmother or novel-chewing
mamma—but H.R.H. has been so profoundly
engrossed in merely admiring the result that he
had to enquire the reason of it, e'ven after 12 years
cogitation. By the way, the Premier’s shoppy
little mot as to “Australian mutton having only
been largely obtainable in England within the
last 12 years,” strikes one as a little illogical.
First, he found English beauty ahead of Austra-
lian, and then he attributed the improvement in
Bull’s daughter to our mutton ! Is even a sheep
not without honour save in his own country?
I hear that it was dismally funny on the morn-
ing the Federal Bank failed —to see the demeanour
of the “society ” depositors (especially the feminine
variety) who thought that in a bank smash, as in
a social function, you can be smuggled in somehow
before the doors are open and have first pick of
what’s going if you happen to be Anybody.
When you know the manager in his evening-dress
moments and everything has been so pleasant, it’s
quite to be expected that he will at least hand
you out your little nest-egg without any fuss—no
matter how impossible lie may find it to pay the
ordinary mob which bangs on the counter and
doesn’t burst into the sanctum. To give the
gentlemen their due, they, I believe, restricted
their illegal endeavour to get ahead of the rest by
putting their excited formula in a feeling way:
“I suppose you can’t get my cash out of the
affair?” But the ladies were more beseeching
and resorted to little sobs, deep sigh's, and the
usual gentle arts which, while they often succeed
in getting money out of the pockets of the male
individual, fall off companies like water off a duck.
One society lady, of rather mature attractions,
who came to pluck her banking-account out of the
burning, had designedly backed herself up with a
pretty girl, in the hope, presumably, that the
manager’s heart might be melted left-handedly.
“Am I iiot to get my money?” (with a 40 h.p.
emphasis on the I), she said, in a high voice, rush-
ing in upon the manager, closely followed by her
decoy-duck. “ You know you can pay me if you
like! Won’t you pay me/” And then, as the
poor man perforce remained obdurate, she
plumped on to a chair. “I shall stay here till I
get my money,” she almost screamed. “ I know
you’ve got it in that safe there, and I won’t
stir until you give it me!” But, of course,
she didn't get it. It’s not a bit of use being pretty
by proxy.
A “risen” Southern family of studied tastes
are awaiting the arrival of an English mail-boat in
a cold perspiration—the family, not the steamer.
On board is a lady relative who wrote before
starting and described the very dress she would
wear on her arrival, so as to lessen the worry of
identification. Here is the description :—“The
dress is red, with dotted about on it little groups,
in shades of blue and yellow, of kangaroos, emus,
lions, unicorns, and true lovers’ knots ”!! ! The
family are praying earnestly that the boat may
arrive long after dark.
The pale, spectacled aristocrat who comes out
to Australia to batten on vice-regal relatives, and
who Seeks to obtain “colonial experience ” with-
out taking off his gloves, is merely a thorn in the
flesh of the long-suffering station-hands most of
the time. One storyette about a certain Governor’s
latest-imported brother-in-law, who went to gain
experience on a Southern station, is a case in
point. He was sent to tally bags of wheat with
the farm bailiff, who left him to count a stack,
and, returning in an hour, asked the unhappy
stripling how many there were? In stammering
tones came the reply : “ I kuk-kuk-eounted up to
one hundred, bub-bub-but got mixed. You’d
bub-bub-better count them your s-s-self!” The
spectacle of an Honorable who can’t keep his
mental grip in the bewildering presence of a
hundred numerals, discounts 10 per cent, of the
convincingness of Onida’s heroes.
I hear that the divorced wife of a well-known
Australian politician is living very quietly just
now near Cambridge Terrace, London. She con-
templates seeking “fresh woods and pastures
new,” and will shed the lustre of her presence on
the Chicago Exhibition. Americans are impres-
sionable and like a pretty woman.
This is the pause of fashion, and the luckless
woman who has to get a new dress would do
wisely to buy a cheap stuff, because nobody knows
how skirts are going. The “ Bell ” skirt—gored
at the top and spreading out towards the hem—a
regular haven for big feet—promises to have a
run, and some people fancy the “granny,” full
all round, but it’s pure guesswork what will lead
local style, and that’s the main point, after all.
What is the go in Paris only makes you look like
a mountebank in Little Pedlington, unless every-
body happens to know about it. But so long as
the new sleeve given in Madame Weigal’s last
journal doesn’t come in, we can hope to be happy.
This horror has a full top ending in a swelled,
pointed bag at the elbow, highly suggestive of
goitre. Fortunately, I only saw one at the cricket
match.
I have thankfully received from Edwards.
Dunlop and Co. a copy of Fashions of To-Day—
a beautifully illustrated paper. It seems a pity
from every but a dress-worshipper’s point of view
that so much artistic work should present merely
the last eccentricity in bonnets and the very latest
snip.
Just as I thought! The awful Brisbane floods
have ended in a wild and savage rush of women
to the bargain counters of the local shops,
“ Damaged by the flood ” will for a year or so be
to the Brisbane tradesmen what “ Salvage ” is to
Sydney, where the water never rises and gives
commerce a chance, and for many months you will
be able to “ spot ” the Brisbane bargain-hunter by
the water-marks on her bonnet-strings. But the
flood damage is a reality and not a delusion.
Brisbane’s leading drapers, Finney, Isles, & Co.,
have, I am told, lost fully =£20,000 through the
rush of water.
If Lord Brassey does get the N.S.W. Governor-
ship, will that high office be lightly considered as
a trap to catch a Sunbeam ?
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Dear Bulletin, —I sincerely hope that
your appeal fot sufficient, fund? td erect a small
Memorial over the grave of Bgrorbft H. Boake
will meet with a sympathetic response. Had hte
lived lie would have made himself a world-famous
name, and interpreted the voice of the Australian
bush worthily and well. What you have already
published of his verses prove him to have pos-
sessed that intuitive knowledge of his kind and
good fellowship with Nature which at once appeals
to the heart and brain of humanity. Fur my
part I think that when he died Australia lost its
coming poet. I should think that all your bush
readers, at any rate, would willingly contribute
something, however small, for such a purpose.—

Dear Bulletin, —“ Bob” Murray, the-
lately deceased telegraph station-master at' the 1
Katherine River on the Overland Line, N.T., was-*
one of the original staff of’that part; He was in
very active fellow, a good' and a keetn
judare of horseflesh. He was the soul of hospi-
tality, and | thy stranger was always welcome atf.
the Katharine. During the big flood of ’Tff, 1 .where
the Katherine rose 35ft. in one night; ho started'
out next morning, crossed the river, and what,
between wading and swimming, ran the line along
towards Pino Greek and restored communication
before nightfall, single-handed. He was a first-
class operator. Many years ago when 1 was on-
the station we were amusing ourselves with cards
one night. Murray was just in the act of playing
when lie stopped and said, “What’s that?” He
put down his cards, went over to the table where
the instrument was ceaselessly ticking, and stood
there jotting down something on a piece of paper-
Then he came hack and read out the news’ of ttie-
defeat and massacre of Isandnla. Most of the-
messages, of course, go through in code, but this-
ono did not, and his quick ear caught the start-
ling tidings speeding through whilst in the act or
playing.-F.

M'Culloch required was old hats at the end of
sticks.”

two weeks’ cruise in “H.M.C.S; Protector.”
This annual jollification costs Kintore nothing,

nant, hard-up citizens want to know how long
the luxury is to last. The Protector has cost
about £150,000 outside the Original outlay;

Editor Bulletin, —One of the largest
retail drapery, etc., establishments in Sydney, has
retrenched to the extent of 12£ per cent, on all
salaries, from the shop - boy upwards. The
upholsterers and such trades belonging to the
establishment, only get work, on an average, for
a day and a half in the week, and are paid accord-
ingly, minus the reduction. Yet the snivel servants
ery out at a proposed reduction of 6 per cent, on
their full-time salary I—E.H.
The utter incompetence of the bulk of the
members of the N.S.W. Labour-party to under
stand varying situations and their duty in each, is
clearly shown by the fact that only three or four
of them assisted the candidature of Fred. Flowers
for South Sydney. About half-a-dozen of the
party compare favourably with any other six men
in the House, but if the balance have any ability
it is carefully concealed beneath mountains of
apathy, laziness, and disinclination for mental
activity.
Zola airily assumes that all public life is
necessarily rotten. In some countries the oorrup
tion is always covered up, and in others it is
brought to light periodically. Political business,
says the French moralist, is in itself a very dirty
oeeu,patron, and will always remain so, wherefore
he “believes i«a the ultimate victory of the
Socialists, but their day has not dawned yet.” In
the opinion of Zola, then, and of ail intelligent
thinkers, it is the decree of Nature that “repre-
sentative ” men must hate the very name .of
Socialism.
Victorian Premier Patterson is going the
way of all the rest. Patterson's one alleged good
quality was that he said what fie meant, and said
it with a yell which was distinctly audible from
the Treasury all the way to Spencer-street railway-
station, that he spoke straight out, and told the
truth, no matter low disagreeable, every time.
Yet, when he appeared at Castlemaine t® explain
his policy, he maundered for two hours and ex-
plained nothing;. It was a mere buzz of plati-
tudes. Even that dheap Sphinx, SI id els, was never
so misty as the once bull-headed man of Castle-
maine.
The Madden (Vic.) Chief Justiceship ap-
pointment makes an amazingly awkward prece-
dent, which has much alarmed some of their
Honors who have hitherto fully expected to be
asked to sit up higher. Puisne Judge Dodds
(Tas.) would not get the Chief Justiceship were it
vacant to-morrow. It would almost certainly go
to Solicitor-General Alfred Dobson, or Inglis-
Clark, the ex- Attornoy-General. Dodds hassome
idea of doffing the ermine and returning to tire
Senatorial Chamber. He had better not. As a
politician he was a mere flash-in-the-pan, and his
short-lived fame is forgotten.
The “ thrift ” vote, inserted by the N.S.W.
Legislative Council in the Elections Act, is an in-
ane absurdity. It gives the landowner a vote
only for property held outside the electorate of his
residence. Thus a wealthy man, owning much
land only in the electorate where he resides, will
have but one vote, while his poor neighbour*
with an allotment outside, will have two. It
only shows the absurdity of the “cash” vote*
Another absurdity of the property vote is that it
may only give a choice of two votes. A man’s
property vote may be so distant from his resi-
dence vote that it will be impossible to exercise
both.
The Electoral Bill, as parsed by N.S.W;
Legislative Council, reduces the proportion of the
property-vote to the total vote from 7.0 per cent;*
at which it has previously stood, to probably less
than two per cent. The Ministry should accept
this reduction and immediately draw up a bill td
absolutely abolish the property-vote; Such a bill
could, Under Parliamentary rules, Hot be dealt
with this session 5 but it could be after a ten days’
prorogation; The issue will then be clear and
distinct—one vote or two. The Council has cut
the wind-pipe of its clients; let the Assembly
now complete the job by slitting the Council’s
Carotid artery;
“Price Waning *’ is henceforth to edit the
Workman (Sydney). The N.S.W. democratic
party had better hurry up and establish a daily
paper of their own—each shareholder to have one
vote and no more. Every daily paper at present
issued in N.S. Wales has over and over again
demonstrated its readiness to “ turn dog ” at the
most critical periods. The existing daily press
appears to represent syndicates and bricks-and-
mortar, not flesh and blood. As we have said
before, the workman puts a penny in the daily
paper’s slot, and gets in return nothing but
the most poisonous insults.
On last censure vote the N.S. Wales
Government had a majority of six—“ Six pounds
a week,” remark the enemies of payment of
members. This time they got a majority
of only three —“Three hundred a year,”
sneers the National Ass, which in its general
election programme includes abolition of pay-
ment to members, or at most the paving
of £lOO a year to each representative. How-
ever, no amount of sophistry will upset the
democratic principle of payment to members,
which is the corollary to universal suffrage. Those
who believe in one must believe in the other, and
vice versa. The National Ass, being vice versa , is
perfectly logical.
That “ overwhelming triumph ” of Patter-
son at the Castlemaine poll was a poor affair. An
old-established fetish in the district and a billet-
provider of long standing, the blatant sectarian
was furthermore appealing to the electors from
the lofty elevation of the Premiership. Patterson,
as Premier, is such a dazzler for Castlemaine that
one would scarcely expect his opponent to poll 20
votes. Yet Longmore, a stranger, with no politi-
cal influence to recommend him to parochial
minds, no good war-cry to raise, and no recent
prestige to stand upon, found 617 supporters as
against the 1441 who shrieked for Patterson.
Considering the “ tons of weight ” that Longmore
was giving away, The Bulletin considers that lie
ran the better horse of the two.
"Only One of Thousands."
Mr, Matthew Laing, clerk, Carrington, who
writes on January 9th, 1893 : —With thousands of
others I feel it my duty to testify to the many
good qualities of Clements Tonic. For the past
three years I have suffered from indigestion, pains
between the shoulders, &c. I have tried the
principal colliery doctors, but their medicines
never had any beneficial effect. One day whilst
sitting at my desk, totting up column after column
of figures, my attention was drawn to a pamphlet
containing some testimonials bearing upon the
curative powers of Clements Tonic, and I tried it
with the gratifying result that after taking two or
three bottles all pain left me, and X felt an
entirely new manand, needless to say, I have
continued to take Clements Tonic, and I would no
more think of going to my work in the morning
without my customary dose than I would
endeavour to convince Sir George Dibbs that he
did not do right in spending that ,£lOOO for his
trip to the .old country. Were Ito write pages of
foolscap £ could not say too much in praise of
Clements. Morning after morning I have arisen
from my bed with a sack and languid feeling,
almost unable to go to my work. As I said before.,
that feeling has now entirely disappeared, and I
can go to my work as fresh as a proverbial daisy-
fit, in fact, to show Treasurer See how to put the
finances of this young country on a sound and
prosperous footing. Already I have recommended
Clements Tonic to scores of my friends, and rest
assured, Mr. Clements, as long as I live and I
have the power to “ wield a quill ” in the interests
of your really wonderful medicine I will do so,
believing as I do that it is the duty of every man
to do so who has been permanently cured. In my
case you have beaten five doctors, and it is with
no end of pleasure that I send you these few lines
of acknowledgment, knowing that you will give
publicity to the fact that I am only one of thou-
sands who have been benefited by your marvellous
Clements Tonic. It is my desire that every
sufferer from indigestion should read this entirely
unsolicited testimonial, and then, and not until
then, will X consider I have done my duty to
suffering humanity. In conclusion, Mr. Clem-
ents, I look upon you as a public benefactor, and
your name will never be'obliterated from my
memory,—Wishing you and your truly marvellous
remedy every success, believe me, yours truly,
Matthew Laing, Colliery Office, Carrington, New
South Wales.
Ayer's Sarsaparilla was the first successful blood
medicine ever offered to the public. This prepar-
ation is still held in the highest estimation both
at home and abroad. Its miraculous cures and
immense sale show this.
The Euoalypte Lozenges for colds, made by
Colemane and Sons, are unsurpassed.

them as freo a man as he had ever been, both
»s regarded financial and other public matters.

coming to the front he did not despair, and he
felt sure that with the earnest co-operation of
the younger generation of politicians which was
rising up, federation would become an accom-
plished fact before many years had passed.

Society
Dear Bulletin, - A delightful piece of
society intelligence appeared in a Sydney paper on
Monday week re Lady Jersey’s malaya (excursion)
to Apolima, one of the Samoan Group. Owing to
the heavy surf running it was impossible for Lady
J. to land, whereat she was “distressed, not only
on her own account but on account of the natives
themselves who had made elaborate preparations
to do honour to the occasion.” To allay the
poignant grief of the darkies of the little crater
isle who had been denied the vision of the Koruna's
fa’c tua, Mr. B. M. Haggard, the British Land
Commissioner (brother, by the way, to “my
brothah Rida.li) ’ was sent last December with
‘Lady Jersey’s portrait, suitably framed, to be
presented as a souvenir of her visit to Samoa.”
Brother Bassett, we are told, was accompanied by
J'ii atulia, the wife of the High Chief Se’umanu-
tafa. It sounds romantic! but the writer well
knows poor, bony, old 1 a’atulia! also her liege lord,
the Bird Snarer, who isn’t High Chief at all. He is
only the local chief of Apia littoral, and Fa’atulia
is, and has been for the past twenty years, the
swell blanchisseuse of Apia—does washing at 2dols.
a dozen, and in soliciting custom displays with
pride her testimonials from various naval officers.
One, in particular, a very tender-looking docu-
meat, is from a U.S. Vice-Consul, stating that
r a atulia, High Chieftainess of Samoa (sic),
washed (sic) the officers of the U.S.S. Resacca
and Jamestown very efficiently.” All the same,
Fa atulia is a very decent old lady, even though
she does occasionally sit in a much undressed
condition up to her stayless waist in the limpid
waters of the Yaisigago and batter the white
folks’ dnds about with a wooden mallet on a
flat slab of coral. And Se’u is a hospitable
old pirate, too, always ready to summon his bevy
of aua luma to chew a bowl of kava for the
str.mgec within his gate. However, it is just as
well that Lady J. didn’t land at Apolima and be
entertained, the which means much feasting,
long-winded, beastly—flattering orations by the
native speakers, and, as a wind-up. a po'ula, or
dance, in which the Taupo, or Maid of the
Vdlage, exhibits her dexterity with a naked mob
of howling young manaia (anglice “bucks”).
Before the dancers are half through, the white
lady visitor generally makes a bolt outside if she
can’t cover her head with her dress or a floor-mat.
Even Commodore Goodenough couldn’t stand the
latter part of a Samoan ballet, but fled in the
midst of the revelry from Matafagatele to Apia,
a good two miles, at dead of night, and vowed
ne’er to look upon such again. Also it may be
safely wagered that the “ picture suitably framed ”
will, after hanging upside-down for six weeks in
the local faipu/e house, be traded away to the
Mmono crowd for three baskets of taro and an
unhealthy pig. Then it will go to Apia, or Mata-
fele, and be swapped for a bar of soap, a tin of
sardines, and two bottles of gin, finally adorning
the walls ol* a local sailors’ boarding-house
kept by some greasy Portuguese individual with
curly hair and ear-rings.—F,

Chief Justice Madden, of Victoria, is to
be formally dined by members of the Bar
“proper,” and no amalgams need apply for
tickets. Thus the Bar Proper seeks to recommend
itself to litigants whose cases are likely to come
before the Chief. “I rise to pulverise the argu-
ments of my inferior learned friend, who, by the
way, was recently debarred from banqueting in
our company, your Honour.”
# # #
A bombshell fell in Hobart Supreme Court
the other day. A sweet young thing of seventeen
summers was being cross-examined as to her
amours with an Assyrian hawker. “ Was he the
only man?” asked Inglis Clark. “No,” said the
girl, looking straight into the well of the Court,
“there was two or three of them lawyer gents—-
’speshly that one witii the bald ’ed.”
* * *
Gov. Glasgow (M.L.) visited Dunedin, the
other day, and overflowed to a deputation about
the old Scotch associations of the city. He said
that its very name awakened pleasant memories
of Caledonia, and that the kilts of the local Rifles
were a comfort to his soul, and the accent of the
people, and the names on the signboards—said
names being chiefly Donald —and the architecture,
and the pibroch of the Hielan’man at the corner
by the Colonial Bank, all made him feel as if he
were once more at home, and even the drizzling
sky was suggestive of the murky shores of the
Hebrides. Then he congratulated the deputation
on their common Scotch origin, and having got
all this off his chest he glanced at the deputation’s
signatures at the foot of the address and found
that they were mostly Solomon, Joel, and Rabbi
Chodowski, also a gentleman named Moss.
There are now no less than six journalists
in Mr. Courtney Smith’s Echo Farm (Sydney)
Home for inebriates. However, the farm isn’t
named, as might unreasonably be supposed, after
Sydney Echo.

# * #
The saddest scene of the Queensland
floods : Three poor women at Ipswich, dripping
wet, trying to dig a hole through to where.their
husbands lay drowned like rats, in a mine-tunnel
30 feet below. The motive was so grand, the
workers so earnest, the pot-holes so pitiful!
The latest nonsense
about the reformation
of society is a novel
by the notorious Lady
Florence Dixie shadow-
ing forth the coming
equality of women with
men—in every way. The
heroine, the daughter of
a divorcee, insists on
being sent to Eton
dressed as a boy! In
this garbshe goes through
her college course, rides
andwins races, goes into
Parliament and leads a
party, &c., &c. This all
to pi-ove that the differ-
ence between women and
men results solely from
their different training.
The book is a mass of
absurdity; where it is
not absurd it is prurient,
and where it is not
prurient it is nas t y.
Consequently, it is likely
to be very popular in-
deed.

A low, common kind
of a correspondent writes
to suggest that it would
tend to materially
strengthen those silken
bonds if one or two of
Meston’s “ Wild Austra-
lian” blacks were married
to sprigs of British
royalty. No doubt, Mes-
ton has a king or two in
his crowd, and if the
princesses took a few
lessons in carrying dilly-
bags and smoking there
seems to be no obstacle
to the union—provided
always that the blacks
are agreeable.

The Argus re the
Melbourne tram trouble:
“ It would be an act of
refined cruelty to suspend
dividends so long as
they cart be paid without
unduly .oppressing the
employes.” At the time
this was written, the
Argus did not consider
10 per cent, off about £2
for 70 hours’ work undue
oppression.
The Queensland floods made good “copy’’
for the papers. An Ipswich reporter recorded
that “a voice which even floods must obey,” said,
“Thus far, and responsive to-day as of old in the
Galilean storm the waters bowed as it were to the
behest of their Creator.’’ This is just like
Ipswich. “As it were” is splendid. A Too-
woomba scribe tried to beat it by picturing Bris-
bane residents gazing on the scene of devastation,
on a pitch-dark night when the gas was cut off,
by the light of “flashes from the foaming river.”

“ Calico is needed for 50,000,000 African
women,” is the burden of a letter just received
in Melbourne from Nyassa, written by Joseph
Booth, a Prahran milkman, who went as mis-
sionary. Evidently 50,000,000 poor women go
about in a ballot poor state.
# # #
A N.S.W. paper publishes a story about a
man who Hanged himself. Being discovered four
hours later he was cut down, and “the usual"
means of resuscitation were employed, but without
success.” The methodical woodenness of the
daily paper reporter looms forth in that last sen-
tence. The man Who Wrote it would rush to put
a hot flannel on the stomach of Raineses the (Treat
when his mummy is unrolled in the museum, in
the hope of bringing the monarch round.

CONVINCING HIS COUSIN.
OUTSIDE PARTY (to up-country Postmaster) : “Would yer be afther
shtamping that parcel whin yer send it off, sir?”
POSTMASTER : “Yes; oh, what does it contain?”
O. P.: “A thermomiter.”
P.: “A what?”
O. P.: “A thermomiter, uv coorse; me cousin down in Sydney won’t
believe that the heat is 110 up here, an Oi’m just sendin’ him
down the thermomiter for him ter see for himself.”
Much adieu about nothing—Gov. .Ter
sey’s departure.

Is there, we wonder, any country on earth
besides Australia where this kind of advertise-
ment is practically kept standing in the daily
press ? These are all out Of one issue of one
paper:—
T HEREBY publicly APOLOGISE lo Miss ELLEN
x lor having- assaulted her and used obscene
language to her, and I also swear that I will not
molest her again from this time henceforth, (Signed)
ALEXANDER .
TF Mrs. does not stop slandering my Daughter,
x I will take proceedings at once. Mrs. .IANE .
I HEREBY warn any person or persons against
miking unfounded and slanderous charges
against me after this date, the same being untrue.
SARAH
"I HEREBY retract the slanderous language I used
against ELKLAND SMITH, the same having no
foundation, in fact. JOHN OSOOTT.
TF Mrs. —— does not stop using foul language to
■* my BOY, I will take proceedings against her after
this date. MARIA .
We meet this sort of thing so regularly in the
Sydney papers, that it would seem as if half the
community spends most of its time abusing the
other half. Why doesn’t the lady who is thus
openly accused of using bad language to the other
lady’s offspring, sue the paper for libel? It almost
always is a lady who has used the language,
by-the-way. When the case lies between two
men, they probably batter each other with road*
metal instead of advertising.

THE PHANTOM VALENTINE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
If Father Time were younger, Flo,
By twenty years or more—
If you were living long ago,
And I, the boy of yore,
In crudest song of “ stars above,”
And ‘‘eyes that brighter shine,"
I would have begged my lady love
To choose her Valentine.
But valentines are not for now,
Old sentiment is dead ;
The lines are deep in Cupid’s brow,
From which his hair has fled.
The day went by-you didn’t know
What joy there was in store
Had you been living long ago,
And I the boy of yore.
I sent a valentine, ’tis true —
In fancy, not in fact;
A lace-edged box, addressed to you,
The lid a little crack’t.
Last Tuesday would have seen it, Flo,
Delivered at your door,
Had you been living long ago,
And I the boy of yore.
I hope you kissed it many times,
And held it to your breast,
And learned by heart its tawdry rhymes
Before you went to rest.
I long to hear you tell me so—
Alas ! the dream is o’er ;
We are not living long ago,
I’m not the boy of yore.
Melb.
EDMUND FISHER.
A Board to track Sir Matthew’s mace,
Ri-tol-de-rol-deray !
Pray will they search each shady place,
And ask a girl with painted face
If she has aught to say
Upon this most suh-rosa case
That has perplexed the human race?
O, cleanse the ’Ouse of all disgrace,
Ri-tol-de-rol-deray !
Now’, if the mace was never seen
(Ri-tol-de-rol-deray !)
By anyone—it can’t have been—
To figure in a festive scene
Since first it went away,
VVhat does a fresh enquiry mean ?
VVe read the statement in between
The lines and cry, “God Save Hie Queen—
Ri-tol-de-rol-deray!”
No gentleman, or lady fair,
Ri-tol-de-rol-deray !
Will come and take an oath and swear
The mace was shifted from its lair
In legislative play.
The world will ne’er be made aware,
The bauble mutt be white-washed—there I
Ri-tol-de-rol-deray !

toute; Much of the slavish nomenclature adopted
is to be placed to the debit of the Geographical

Thomas Elder will send Lindsay out again, but
he won’t have anything to do with the Geographi-
cal Society this time. Even the womankind of
the members of the Council have been honoured
in knoll, glen, creek, hill, and range, the only
member whose name is omitted from the map
being Mr. Bonython, proprietor of the Adver-
tiser, and thereby hangs an amusing tale which
may one day be told. The range which Lindsay
named after Forrest is called “ Einlayson ” (pro-
prietor of S. A. Register) on the map, and a little
mount close by is christened after his wife (Alice)
—another alteration. Kyffin Thomas (of the
Register) has a bilk
Editor Bulletin, —The foundation-stone
of a new hospital has just been laid on the
Murray by Masons, and with Masonic ceremony.
Is this fair to the Catholics who contributed tc
that hospital? Is it fair to the general rate-
payers of the province, who have contributed 8(J
per cent, of the cost up to now ? Is it fair to the
contributing men, like myself, who hate Masons
and Oddfellows, and Hibernians, because they
wear aprons and bibs and sashes and such-like
old womanities ? B.
* * *
Dear Bulletin, —Your Queensland corres-
pondent, in re sugar-making by lepers, reminded
me of some North Australian experiences of
mine. At a certain plantation, when refining
was going on, you could see naked aboriginals
standing knee - deep in the vats shovel-
ling out molasses into the refiner. If these
noble savages could not expectorate out of the
vats from where they stood—well, they had to
spit into them. And North Australia has pro-

A (Mabriland) love-story, late in life. An
old soldier and wife returning to their suburban
home, after drawing his pension and spending
'Portions of same at various wayside pubs. Scene,
after leaving last hostelry; time, about 8.30 p.m.
Pensioner (beerily)Come on, my dear.’’ Wife
(savagely): “Don’t call me your dear, you old
beast!” Pensioner (with military intonation):
“Come on, my devil.” The next scene is full of
sticking-plaster.— Waitemata.

# * *
One morning the week before last a Sydney
merchant advertised for a clerk. By 3 p.m. he
had received 132 applications, 98 delivered per-
sonally, 34 by post; 120 of the applicants were
apparently competent, and the best was selected.
He claimed proficiency in shorthand, English,
French, and German. His credentials covered 20
years of European and Australian experience—
and lie offered to work for 355. per week. The
average commercial experience claimed by the
applicants was 12 years. Twenty - eight appli-
cants claimed Continental or British experience,
four said they could speak and write English
and French ; three English and German; three
English, French, and German; one English,
French, and Spanish; and another English, Ger-
man, and Hutch ; and five on their credentials
were shorthand and type-writers. Five said they
had had businesses of their own which the bad
times had ruined ; 40 definitely stated their ages—
the youngest was 19, the oldest 43 years; the
average age was 29 years. One-half of the appli-
cations were written on good note or business
paper, one-third on paper of poor quality ; 10
came on pieces of foolscap, eight on Coffee Falace
note, one on a butcher’s bill, one on an Iron-
monger’s, and one on the bill of a timber-merchant.
Ten said that at time of writing they were in
temporary employment; three said they were
single; six, each with first-class credentials.
Owned to the married state. All the married
applicants had a sentence like this : “I have A
wife and family and I will gladly commence on a
small salary.” Seventeen others, 12 having excel-
lent references, were willing to take wages rang-
ing from £2 2s.- to 255. per week. “Being
destitute, I will be thankful to accept any offer.”
“As being out of work does not suit my consti-
tution, I will gladly take 255. per Week.” “ I will
take, any salary and Work any hours.” One
applicant said he had been “ honoured with the
predicates gifted and industrious,” and yet he
“ now toiled 85 hours per week for a butcher.”

Bear Bulletin, — He. the 4t warrigal con-
troversy,” let me say that the word “warrigal”
was current aboriginee ere the British Lion
deposited “our forefathers’’ here. Since then,
the word has been applied, both by blacks and
whites, to anything wild, from the warrigal steer
to the warrigal cabbage, exactly as the familiar
combination “old-man” is used to denote any-
thing large or formidable, from a kangaroo to a
swindle, from a species of salt-bush to a hammer-
ing ; or the adopted corruption, “ picanniny,”
to denote anything small or insignificant, such as
the church-collection currency. But during the
idyllic era of Australia—extending from the year
1 A.M. to 1788 A.D. —“warrigal” signified “out-

Signor Nerli, just returned from Samoa,
after a three months’ sojourn in that Paradise of
the Pacific, has not been idle. The numerous
landscapes he has brought back with him show
that he has distinctly caught the peculiar atmos-
pheric effect of the tropical climate in the South
Sea islands. This is specially noticeable in his
view of Apia; and his painting of a banana-grove
when the fruit is flowering, with the half-length
portrait of a native in the foreground, shows that
the artist is equally at home in landscape and
figures. But, beyond question, the best work
done by Signor Nerli during his visit to Samoa
is his portrait of Louis Stevenson, the novelist.
This is a remarkably fine piece of painting, the
■strange, dreamy expression of the clever brown
eyes being admirably caught; the broad, firm
handling of the subject is beyond praise; In this
instance Nerli evidently had a subject worthy of
his brush, and even his greatest admirers in Syd-
ney, who know only tdo well that he has been in
the habit of wasting his powers irt putting
on canvas representations of nonentities, who
look like nonentities, will be surprised at
this manifestation of his skill. It is to
be hoped that his Samoan experience will lead
him to attempt more earnest work than goes to
the production of pot-boilers, over which he has
frittered away his undoubted talent only too
long.
Spoug’s new drop - scene at Sydney
Criterion is a meritorious work of art, especially
when one takes into account that the painting
was bound to follow out the somewhat novel
scheme of decoration adopted in the new theatre.
From time immemorial, for some inscrutable
reason, the only style held to be suitable for a
play-house was that of the Renaissance, but the
decorator who was entrusted with the embellish-
ment of the Criterion determined to discard
tradition, and there can be no ’doubt that the
result has justified his decision. Hence we have
at least one playhouse in Australia for which it
may be fairly claimed that the interior decoration
is distinctly original and a scheme of colour and
design carefully and conscientiously worked out.
Mr. Spong chose for his subject a courtyard in a
Moorish palace. The bright blue sky in the back-
ground serves to throw into relief the rich reds
and greys of the intricate architecture in the
arches, while the yellow floor, with the geometrical
tiles, gives the necessary light to the whole. The
figures, which were painted by Mr. J. M'Cubbin,
of Melbourne, are unquestionably above the
average, more especially the dancing female,
in the centre of the composition. On one point
Mr. Spong is to be particularly complimented.
He has made his picture fill the whole of the can-
vas, instead of, as is usual in the large majority of
drop-scenes, being satisfied with painting an oval
or square, surrounded with lace, fillagree, or satin
curtains, which, however clever they may be from
a technical standpoint, are artistically meaning-
less. It is to be hoped that this new departure
will form a precedent for those scene-painters who
may be called upon to produce drop-scenes for
Australian theatres.

THE WOOING O’ ’T,
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
He was a bachelor, gallant and gay
She was a spinster prim—
Pretty and prim, with a wonderful way
Which had captivated him.
Oh ! well knew she what he wished to say,
So—never frigid nor freezy—
Molly M.iginnis managed that day
To make his saying o’’t easy.
“Bob, I shall get you a wife,” said she,
“Find some nice, dear girl for you ;
Bob! please tell me the sort she must be,
Shall her eyes be brown or blue?
“ Must she be of the ‘ plump and the pleasing ’ sort ?
Will the 1 slender and willowy ’ do?”
Here Robert the Bachelor, cutting her short,
Said ; “ She must be just like you !
“And to me, sweetheart, ’tis distinctly clear,
There is none in the world like you.”
This fell in the fall of the finished year,
They are married now in the New.
Warrego.

A FRIENDLY WARNING.
L. L., old party,
Though loud and hearty.
With spiciest anecdotes and such replete.
There’s one thing lacking
If you’d be tracking
To Marlborough House to give Wales a treat,
Your style and diction
Will cause no friction,
And Blue Blood will roar when you wink yottr eye,
But you’ll drive ’em mad, sir,
And they’ll call you cad, sir,
If you move among ’em witKout e’er d He.

the M.C.C. “cracks,” Mr. Coningham, of the kangaroo
methods, will very probably come out just about top of
the bowling list, and perhaps of the batting list also.

George Giffen’s last three innings : 185, 172 not
out, and 136. Yet his last innings of 136 brings his club
average down from 199 to 178 for three completed innings.
# # #
George Giffen gets 12 months’ leave from S.A.
Government without pay. On his last tour he received
full pay “owing to the advertisement to the colony.”

Charley Bannerman says that Coningham is the
best bowler in Australia.

Graham’s last innings in Sydney proved him to
be what A. Bannerman and Gregory said of him—“ a per-
fect batsman.”

If the mistakes made by fieldsmen were noted
in the scoring book it would do much to simplify the task
of ciioosing interproviucial and international cricket
teams. In American baseball, every time a man misses a
ball within bis reach counts as an error against his name.
• * #
The Australian boxing boom is dying. Like
pedestriaiiism and sculling, it has had its day—and has
done more for blackguardism than either.
« * •
Entries for the V.R.C. Autumn Meeting are
below- the average in quantity and quality. Such a
“classic ” race as the Loch Plate has only three first-class
horses on the bill—St. Hippo, Camoola, and Autonomy.
• • *
It must be fifteen years since Hanlan first
astonished the Britishers in a sculling-match on the Tyne,
and now the poor old young man is going over to pit his
ghost against Bubear. Probably the very second-rate
Bubear will “ do him bad.”
* * *
Otto Hug’s (East Melbourne) St. Bernard bitch
Empress has littered 10 pups by Lord of the Manor, a le-
centlylimported dog, bred by Jno. Speight, of Bingley,
Yorkshire. Lord of the Manor is about the biggest Sc.
Bernard ever seen in this country, and is just three years
old. He has only been shown twice (in England), and
secured prizes on both occasions. The sire was Plin, and
his grandsire Plinlimmon, who was sold to an American
fancier for 1000 guineas. The latest descendants of Plin
are fairly-built puppies, and are a distinct acquisition to
the stud dogs of Australia.
# # #
Why don’t they run Melbourne steeplechases on
the old English donkey-racing plan ? Every man to ride
another man’s nag, and the last past the post takes the
prize. It would promote straight running, anyhow.
# # *'
Bowls are not in such fig in Melbourne as they
were when Beaney and Mark Moss used to vie in the mag-
nificent trophies they got from the Denis silversmiths.
# # #
The N.S.W. Assembly wouldn’t have the
Totalisator Bill at any price, and dismissed it ignomini-
ously. The hill, by the w ay, was vigorously opposed by
Labour-member Andy Kelly, now a distinguished orna- j
ment of Tattersall’s Club.
* • *
A disqualified-for-ever galloway mare spent a
pleasant and lucrative racing season in the North, not
long hack. Which recalls the fact that a pony, tied up
for 12 months in Melbourne, raced at Brisbane during his
enforced retirement from Victoria, and starting as a
maiden finally won about a dozen races. When his time
was up he returned South. And perhaps the fastest
American trotter that ever came to Australia went North
and under a new’ name ran as a maiden and won.
* • *
There would be no reason for the Victorian
jockeys to form a union in order to recover riding fees
from “long-winded” owners were the Y.R.C. to formu
late a rule similar to No. 29 of the English Jockey Club,
viz.: “ A horse shall not start for a race unless there has
been duly paid before weighing out the jockey’s fee,
under rule 53.” The last-named rule merely refers to
amount of fees, &c.
# * *
Paddy Q., a Newcastle cricketer, bowling at a
clergyman, shivered his leg stump. “My G [’’ex-
claimed Pat, throwing up his arms with a howler’s
demonstration. “Mind your stops,” said his rev. “So
help me —— ! it nearly howled you,” said the irrepres-
sible. Which recalls how a leading English bowler was
ordered off the field for the remark, as he clean-bowled
"parson ” Studd—“ There goes his pulpit!”
• * #
Many persons in Australia to whom he was not
personally known will regret to hear that Lord Londes-
borough is very seriously ill. A more open-hearted,
generous man never lived, and his kindness to Trickett
and Laycock, when they were at low W’ater in England,
will surely never be forgotten (says our London corres-
pondent) by those who knew the circumstances of the
case. He found the whole of Laycock’s stakes when Elias
rowed and beat Boyd on the Tees ; and when Trickett was
so ill after the Hop Bitters regatta, when he rowed in a
snow-storm, Londeshorough had him and his trainer
Kelley down to stay at Lyndhurst, where he put a cottage
at their disposal, with servants, carriage and coachman.
He also found part of Trickett’s stake when the latter
rowed Kirby at South Hampton, and he took about £5O
of steamer-tickets for the race, and brought a large party
of friends from London.
# # #
Mr. “ Billy ” Auslow may now take his place
with the evergreens. At Manly, on Saturday, the old
Sydney R.C. oarsman won the Swimming Championshin
of the Village.
* * *
Mr. Clarence Hannell, of Newcastle, thinks of
taking a health-trip to Europe. Newcastle sport and
Clarence Hannell have walked hand in hand for many a
long day. Mr. Hannell was the champion amateur oars-
man of his generation, and there is a tradition in the
city of black diamonds that in a race on one occasion the
burly Clarence pulled the top-streak clean out of the
skiff he was rowing. Next!

Joe Thompsm, aided by a few second-class
hookies, and a section of the “professional backer”
element (the curse of the English turf), succeeded in
defeating R. 11. Pry’s at tempt to get the Jockey Club to
license bookmakers. It was thought that the amount
produced by the license might have been devoted to the
payment of a special body of police to protect the
habitiffis of Tattersall’s ring from the raids of the black-
guards who somehow contrive to obtain admittance. This
successful opposition of Joe’s will not tend to enhance
Fry’s love for him. It is thought that Joe was most ill-
advised to take so prominent a part against a movement
which had, tub rosa, the approval of the Jockey Club.
Jack Hall has been beaten in America. When
Hall was at his best in Australia, he was at the top of the
lightweights, but was too uncertain a personage to inspire

The irony of fate. When the victorious
French entered King Behanzin’s capital the first
rush was for the palace where the famous “ golden
throne ” of Dahomey was waiting to be plundered.
The palace was undefended, and the golden throne
was captured. It proved to be a wooden chair
covered with gold varnish, and when the captors
turned it upside-down with a view to mashing it
furiously against the pavement they found under-
neath the legend, “ Made in Germany.”
Amongst the passengers for Sydney by
the Himalaya is Captain (Militia) E. W. Mar-
shall, son of the late Frank Marshall, well-known
as a dramatic critic and playwright, who was a
bosom friend and “pal” of Clement Scott, 25
or more years ago. Frank Marshall married, en
second noces, the then popular actress, Ada
Cavendish, who is not, however, the mother of the
young, rich, unmarried and good-looking globe-
trotter. .Young Marshall possesses one of the
best private menageries in the world, and his col-
lection of pheasants is unique in its completeness.
He used to walk about at Marlow (where he has a
house) followed bv a huge lion cub, to the great
perturbation of the peace-loving inhabitants of
that sleepy little Thames-side town. He is just
too late for the circus.
London edition of Detroit Free Press
now gives small prizes for smart original para-
graphs. Appended is one of the prize-takers. It
is really an excerpt from Victor Daley’s “Be-
tween Two Bottles,” written for The Bulletin
years ago. In our mind’s eye we see Jamie Nicol,
of Glaisnock-st., Cumnock, Ayr, passing off the
Song of Solomon on a music-hall manager for a
new patter turn :
A Cumnock man gives the world his scheme for be-
coming a teetotaller. He said to a friend : “ I feel a lot
better since I took those two drinks. After all, it is
absurd nonsense for a man, when he has been drinking
‘to put a plug in’ suddenly. More than that, it is
dangerous. I read in this morning’s paper of a man who
dropped dead through having—after a long bout-given
up his liquor all at once. I can tell you it is a warning to
m?. The thing should be done gradually. Say six drinks
on Monday, five on Tuesday, four on Wednesday, getting
down to one (big one) on Saturday. So beginning and
ending each week until you end it altogether.”— J.N
Glaisnock-strect, Cumnock, Ayr.
I Mayor Manning, Protectionist candidate
for South Sydney, was returned last Monday by a
majority of 23 over Freetrade Foxall, and at
latest advices all the Freetrade press was busy
explaining that this was a “ virtual ” defeat for
the Government. As for the Labour candidate
the melancholy apathy of a party without a
leader left him nowhere —he received 814
votes against no less than 2017 polled by the
same man at last general election. If the
party had possessed a capable leader the results
might have been different, for it is the first busi-
ness of a leader to get as many followers as
possible. As it is, it was the interest of no Labour
member in particular to organise the campaign,
and among the dozen or more who want the
leadership the addition of a new supporter might
only mean a new rival for the coveted billet.
Therefore, it is quite possible that some of them
would as soon see a new Labour candidate de- I
feated as successful. As a matter of fact, a lead- I
mg Trades-hall official openly worked for the I
successful candidate, }

Dear Bulletin. —lt is quite true that the
body of Richard O’Hara Burke was buried at
Innaminoka (S. A.), but, together with that of his
companion Wills, it now lies under a magnificent
block of granite in Melbourne General Cemetery.
When quite a little boy, away back in 1863, I
tolled a school-bell in Beechworth, where Burke
was once a police magistrate, on the day when his
funeral was taking place in Melbourne. I also
stood beside the open grave of his fellow-explorer,
King, who was buried some years afterwards in
the Wesleyan portion of Melbourne Cemetery.
The inscription on the tree at Innaminoka was cut
by Burke himself before he lay down to die under-
neath it.—Yours, &c,, W.
“ New Australia ” item by our old friend
“John Miller,” of Brisbane Worker :
For it is not political bonds that bind men together ; it
is the common thoughts we think, and the common
tongue we speak, and the common aspirations that are
ours. And when in our Paraguayan villages our boys
and girls woo each other in our own dear mother-tongue,
sweeter to us in its harsh strength than the softer utter-
ance of Castilian speech ; and when we drink our fill
from the matchless poetry of our people, and in our old
age ponder there over the sublime thoughts which are
open to every man who speaks with English lips; and
when we sing our English songs, and work in English
ways, and have learnt to live again in the stern, strong,
English fashion, far off though we be, we shall be Aus-
tralian still—Australian of the Australians, as here we
could never be. The same stars, even, will gleam down
upon us. The very sky will be still of the Australian
blue. To and fro between us all kindly words will pass
and repass for the sake of the blood-tie between us,
blotting out all present irritations, but never blotting
out the heart-felt remembrance of those true friends who
shook our hands and blessed us in our going.
When we read this it seemed to remind us of
something, and after thinking all night it sud-
denly dawned upon us what that something was
—lt was General Scadder selling allotments in the
City of Eden. Meanwhile, and altogether apart
from the memories of Scadder, The Bulletin
wishes to point out that the above eloquence is a
hunk of black ingratitude. The Paraguayan
Government has agreed to take in the “New
Australia ” wanderers, to give them a decent-
sized principality gratis, and to be a brother to
them generally. And in return, instead of throw-
ing in their lot for better or worse with the people
who took them in, they propose to adhere through
all the ages to the country which, they say, threw
them out. The least the colonists can do is to
become Paraguayans heart and soul, to forget the
“mother-tongue” and the “ poetry,” and to give
up pondering over the sublime British thoughts
above-mentioned, and to forget that the sky is
Australian blue, and remember that it is Para-
guayan blue instead. No man, not even a “New
Australian,” can serve two masters.

“I am 55 years of age, and until 1884 had al-
ways enjoyed good health. About that t me, a
little hard bunch, like a wart, appeared on my
lower lip. Finally, the bunch gathered, broke,
and formed a sore ; then it began to eat into the
flesh. The Cancer—for that is what the doctors
finally called it—kept getting worse and worse,
year after year. It caused me great pain and
uneasiness. During the last year, I would wake
twenty times in a night, suffering intensely
from twitching pains. It was as if my flesh
was being torn from me by pinchers, bit by bit.

In the course of a week or two I noticed a
decided improvement. The twitching and the

began to heal, and, after using the Sarsaparilla
for six months, the last trace of the Cancer dis-
appeared, and left very little scar.”—J. E. Nich-

IT’S GONE, ALL GONE, AND I'M GOING
TOO.
Foe many a year did the same man sweep a
pertain street-crossing in Hampstead. Through
0,11 seasons and in all weathers, there he was,
sweeping the crossing and taking such gratuities
7 were given him. Time wore away, and ho came
to be eighty years old. Ho appeared at his post
no more! Aladv district visitor looked him up at
his lodgings. What a picture of squalid destitu-
tion No” fire, no food, no friends. Wife and
family he had nono-never had. The poor old
fellow was perishing of starvation, of want. Some
money was raised for his benefit and he was re-
moved to a London hospital. Here ho lay several
weeks, sinking daily. One night he was clearly
very low. Near him stood one of the hospital
nlivsiciaiis and a nurse. Seeing him clutch
nervouslv at his pillow, the nurse, supposing the
natient desired to be raised up, put his arm
beneath him to perform that service. In doing
„ t [ le nurse’s hand came in contact with an
obiect which he withdrew. It was a dirty little
canvas bag tied with a leather string. As it was
laid aside the old man perceived what had been
done lifted his skeleton frame partially from the
bed and, trembling with excitement, said in a
shrill whisper, “Ah ! my treasure, my treasure !
It’s gone, all gone, and I’m going too !” and sank
back' 5 dead. The bag contained <£soo in notes—
the savings of his miserly life. And he, there,
dead of starvation, even more than of age. Well,
what of it ? yon say. The wretched old man was
better dead than alive. Quite so, but most
human events have a moral, a lesson, about them,
if we keep an eye out for it.
What, for example, can we learn from the
following facts ?—One night about ten years ago a
man, w I lose name we can furnish, went to bed as
usual, apparently in good health and spirits. A
few hours later he lay unconscious on the floor.
In explanation he stated that he had been seized,
suddenly, with a pain of such violence that he was
compelled to rise—a pain in the chest. After
rising he lost his senses and sank down on the
spot°where he had stood. His wife, aroused by
the noise, struck a light and saw her husband in
that situation. She afterwards declared he had
gone black in the face, and that his eyes looked as
if they were starting out of his head.
Restoratives wore applied which brought him
to, but he was not as before. So quickly and un-
expectedly do we cross the boundary line between
two opposite bodily conditions. It is like
stepping from the broad blaze of day into a damp
cavern packed with darkness. He felt weak and
sick, with a strange “all-gone” sensation through-
out ’his whole system. His mouth tasted badly,
and was filled with a slimy sort of phlegm, his
head ached, lie was unable to draw a deep breath,
lie walked with difficulty, and went about his
business like a man who is haunted by a para-
lysing dream.
Perplexed and alarmed he consulted physicians,
who prescribed for him, without, however, pro-
ducing any noticeable improvement. The strong,
clear-headed man of previous years was gone—
changed as by the wand of a vicious magician into
the feeble being he now was. Even with this
dismal prospect before him our friend travelled
not on level ground ; his path led downward ; he
grew worse. In December, 1888, he had a dis-
tinct and bad attack, gave up business, and went
to bed. There he remained for a weary painful
month—Unity days, as long as thirty years of
power and occupation. The doctor said there was
something wrong with the stomach and bowels.
After ho once more rose from his bed he still
suffered dreadful pain and could rest neither day
or night. Indeed, some nights he never slept a
moment. So weak had he become that when he
attempted a short walk he was obliged to
don the effort, return and go to bed.
His own words are these: —“To give you an
idea how reduced I had become I may mention
that I lost over three stone weight and was wast-
ing away, I kept on like this until January,
1891, when Mr. Everson, of Occold, told me of a
medicine called Mother Seigel’s Syrup and the
good it had done. I tried it and in three days I
felt bettor. Cheered and encouraged by this I
continued to use it, with the result that I wholly
recovered from my mysterious malady. lam now
strong and hearty, and business is again a
pleasure. The Syrup did me more good in a few
weeks than all my ten years doctoring put to-
gether.” (Signed) Albert Thorndyke, Proprietor
of the “Grapes Inn,” Church Street, Eve,
Suffolk. May Ist, 1891.
What do we learn from this? We learn that
while a miserly fool like our crossing-sweeper may
starve for money, a wise man with more reverence
for his bodily temple, seeks and finds a remedy
for a tendency to starvation, induced by disease^ —
that the disease was indigestion and dyspepsia,
and the remedy Mother Seigel’s Syrup.
Special Biographies.
(FOR THE BULLETIN. )
There’s some sense in Hazei.l’s Annual. I have
just looked up Dibbs and Parkes. How nicely
the compiler has struck on the salient points of
the lives of these great men !
I don’t care much about politics, but I wish to
balance the pair and find which is the more
remarkable. Judging by points, and the maximum
being 100, I refer to my Hazkll and give marks
as follows:
“George Dibbs has served twelve mouths in prison.”
(N.B.—Tub Bulletin has also been there.)—Marks, 20.^
“ He received the honour of knighthood in ’92.”
Deduct those 20 marks.
“ He is an eminently handsome man.”—Marks, 50.
“ G.R.D. teas a Republican.”—Marks, 50.
“ G.R.D. is now not a Republican.” —Minus, 100.
Result, nil.
Now for Parkes.
“Parkes is the son of an agricultural labourer.” —
Nothing for that: not his fault.
“ He has been distinguished for his successful advocacy
of Freetrade.”—Nix for that: not true.
“ He is one of the remarkable men who have risen to
political eminence through journalism.” (What does
Milton say of Satan ? “By merit raised to that had emi-
nence.”)—Give him credit for 50 marks.
“ His autobiography ” —Forfeit his good-conduct
badges.
But the last item, taken with the first sentence, is
good.
(а) “Was born in 1815.”
(б) “A son was born to him in September - , ’92.’*
Subtraction sum—92 minus 15, equal to 77.
Judging the pair as I would sheep, or horses, or
game fowls, or pointers at a show, I should give
them, each of them, a leather medal with nicks,
as first-class shufflers.

When the s.s. Ruahine’s freezing-chambe 1 ’
was opened at Townsville, the apparently dead
body of a fine collie dog was found lying against
the door, at which, in life, it had vainly
scratched to attract attention. It was dragged
out .and thrown on the wharf, where, in the
tropical heat, it soon thawed, and, to everyone’s
astonishment, after being in the sun for about an
hour the dog feebly raised its head and began to
moan. Restoratives were applied, but poor Tyke
died of exhaustion a few hours afterwards. The
dog belonged to Brisbane and had accidentally
been shut in.

affection of the Nerves, which affected me so much that

Doctors and many Remedies, but all of no avail. Whereas,

PURELY HERBAL f ‘ UNIVERSAL ALFALINE
FILLS,” which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare

this solemn declaration, conscientiously believing the same to

THOS. MUDIMAN.

"The Greatest of Australia’s Dead."
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Non onmiu mortar, multaque pars mei
Vitablt Libitinam
Horace Odes, Bk. HI., 30.
There is a life that buds but after death,
Then quickens, careless of the idle tomb
Where sycophants lie gathered in the gloom
With those poor gains their treason purchaseth ;
So when a hero dies, 10, each one saith :
“This narrow space is neither time nor room
For such a soul to reach its fullest bloom.
His life has not gone out with mortal breath.”
Then, look not back, nor idly shed a tear
For him who’s gone, nor cypress glean, nor rue,
But strive we stalwart with him, onward, who
Won wreaths that fade not and can never sere,
That, should the nation call, “ Are any true ?”
Again; that we may say “Yes ! some are here.”
Lane Cove.
A. C. FONTAINE.
From report of the proceedings at the
Richmond (N.S.W.) Borough Council From
Henry B , drawing attention to the condition
of Mr. W. J. Kelly’s pig-sty, near his garden.

“ Tommy Walker,” the best known of the
few aboriginals who now pervade Adelaide,
astonished a gaping crowd the other day by com-
plaining thus : “ It’s a strange thing that a (Aus-
tralian adjective) gentleman can’t walk down the
street without being followed by a lot of damned
larrikins.” It was Tommy Walker when
asked by Stock, M.P., how he came to be travel-
ling in a first-class railway carriage, answered:
“Just the same as a member of Parliament:
got a free pass.”
What a State Church can lead to was
shown in the case of Read v. the Bishop of
Lincoln, which was decided by the Privy Council,
in 1892. It was decided that although mixing
wine with water during the Communion service is
an offence against the law, and savours of Popery,
yet using a cup containing wine and water which
had been mixed before the service is quite legal
and sound Protestantism; and, further, that
lighting candles on the altar during a day-light
service is an abomination which must be stopped,
but if the candles are lighted before the service
begins, everything is 0.K., and the congregation
has no right to howl. It cost about <£3ooo to
settle these important matters, and the Church
feels much better now.
The N.S.W. country editor goes for his
loathsome contemporary once more :
What old journalistic fornicator is it that stole a man’s
wife 16 years ago and passed her off as his own? What
snuffle-busting old cur is it that, while living as a double-
edged adulterer and bristle-haired fornicator, became a
churchwarden, and palmed another man’s wife on to the
toffs’ wives as bis own church-blessed lovey-dovey, and
then, when the parson-bowled them out as a broomstick
family, persuaded the poor wretch of a woman to commit
bigamy by marrying him? Who is it; can anyone tell?
He, the old dog, he is a nice cup of tea to talk about
people. The drunken old gutter-snipe, the cowardly old
skunk ;he is not game to fight like a man—he never was
half a man. He has always been a pretendedly sancti-
monious old hypocrite, who has made his living by journa-
listic prostitution. Pooh ! get out, you filthy old dog,
you stink in the nostrils of decency !
This is equal to the very best American, and re-
minds one of Parliament.
The city of Ban Salvador (Central America)
is reported to have had two or three earthquakes
recently. San Salvador is a curious and unsettled
city. It has been destroyed three times, and
once it was visited by 80 earthquakes in 60
minutes. On that occasion a monument which
stands in the principal square was shifted 30 yards
and left standing. The ground on which San
Salvador stands is hollow—it is planted on the
thin roof of a series of stupendous caverns, and if
the roof ever goes the city will never stop falling
till it reaches Tophet. About a year ago a man
was digging a well there, and he went through
the thin crust and vanished. He has never been
heard of since. Whether he fell 1000 feet or 1000
miles is a matter of conjecture. Yet a careless,
joyous race lives and dances and makes love and
takes its best girl out at night on the sheet of
tissue-paper which hangs over the great vacancy,
and no one seems to worry in the least about the
void below.
Old Sproule lived by himself ou a selection
40 miles from Bowen, N.Q. The other day, for
the first time in over 20 years, he put up at a pub,
in town for the night, and being of temperate
habits went to bed at his usual time—dark,
About nine o’clock the publican’s dogs started a
row in front of the “ loose boxes,” as the detached
bedrooms are called, and half awoke the old man,
who ran into the main street shouting, “ Sool him,
Snap! Sool him, boy !” under the impression
that he was at home, and that dingoes were at his
fowls again. A yell of laughter from the hotel
verandah and half-a-dozen female shrieks having
thoroughly awakened him, he grasped the situa-
tion firmly with both feet, and made straight for
the bush. Next afternoon a stockman who had
met him 35 miles out, attired simply in a Orimean-
shirt, left word that Sproule’s horse and swag
were to be sent to Burke’s Crossing by first team.
Visions of arrest for indecent behaviour troubled
the old man for some time, and he had a couple of
blacks camped at the “turn off” for weeks so
that he might be duly apprised on the approach of
a trooper.

Business oracle: “ Property has depreci-
ated so enormously, sir, that the richer a man was
two or three years ago the poorer he is now.”
Somebody says of Labouchere that he has
such a hatred of humbug that he would kick him-
self if he were able to reach the right spot with
his boot.
The Bulletin’s current Missing Word
competition (for particulars of which vide page 15)
closes this Saturday, 18th Feb. The result will
be announced in next issue.
Judge Boucaut (S.A.), at present in Eng-
land, is most ec momical. In a recent S.A.
Advertiser article he says that a London waiter
absolutely refused—monstrous !—to accept the
penny tip the Judge offered him !
The other day in Melbourne a young man
went to the police station and asked the help
of the police to find out his name. He had entirely
forgotten it, also where he lived and who he was.
The authorities could not help him, and the
doctors certified him sane and sober. He is in
the gaol hospital endeavouring to arrive at a solu-
tion of his identity.
We were in a Sydney mercer’s shop one
day last week when a Chinaman called in. He
carried a doubtful-looking singlet in his yellow
hand, and explained that he had bought it there a
few days previously and now wanted to change it
because it was “too tightee.” The transaction,
as it happened, didn’t come off, but The Bulletin
would be glad to know the address of any mercer
who habitually changes underclothing for China-
men in order that it may warn the public to keep
clear of him.
Spagnoletti; “ One of your galls, travelling
in England, is afforded by the trespass notices,
but a beautiful English feeling comes o’er you,
like a myriad-minded vision of imagination, when
you travel by the Y.R.C. racecourse, Flemington,
and see the abundant notices to trespassers.
What is this but Byron Moore’s try-on, paving
the way to a charge for admission ? Good old
Fitz Gibbon has always been the best friend to the
people, resisting the encroachments on reserves,
parks, and gardens.”
A tired-looking woman, runs the story,
managed to obtain a private interview with a
London sassiety man, and promptly began to
worry him for the loan of a “fiver.” The male
personage, however, was adamant, and after
grovelling on her knees in vain for a considerable
period, the weary fragment retired to the gentle-
man’s sofa with the avowed intention of taking
poison from a small black bottle. “ I will die
here,"’ she murmured, as she slowly removed the
cork. The sassiety man cashed up the necessary
sum, and his melancholy visitor went silently
away to the capitalist next door. It is supposed
that they must be making at least .£3OOO a year
by threatening to leave her corpse in the mansions
of the rich. Also there is & unanimity of opinion
that the “ fragment’s ” fee for departing in a live
state is extremely moderate.
