“Bully" Hayes, the Pirate of the Pacific.
A CHAPTER IN SOUTH SEA HISTORY.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
No. 11.
Before detailing my own first experiences
of the 18-stone pirate I must mention some other
incidents in his career which will give a fair
illustration of the notoriety he had acquired and of
his keen sense of humour. Long before these two
gentlemen (Bully Hayes and Ben Peese) had com-
menced to exploit the Ellice, Gilbert, Kingsmill,
Marshall and Caroline Groups, Bully, then owner
of a small, fast-sailing schooner, had made unto
himself a name—particularly as a connoisseur of
Island beauty—among the Marquesas, Society,
Hervey and Paumotu Groups, from Nuka-hiva to
Rapa-nui (Easter Island), that ethnographical
mystery of the Southern Seas, whose gentle and
amiable people, 27 years ago, met with so dreadful
a fate at the murderous hands of the Peruvian
slavers.
• * *
Soon after the slavers had gone a story
was current in Eastern Polynesia that Bully had
landed armed boats’ crews at Aana, in the
Paumotu Archipelago, and seized a number of
girls whom he sold to Chilian and Peruvian
buyers. But, as a matter of fact, Hayes never
sold a native girl,.though he was always willing to
barter for a new charmer any member of his harem
who had palled upon his fastidious tastes. And
if the other man in these little matters evinced the
slightest want of trade-reciprocity, he generally
regretted it, for he would lose the household
chattel and get nothing for her, save, perhaps, a
bad pounding and many lumps and excoriations,
or, perhaps, a sarcastic note informing him that
the writer could not afford to waste time haggling
over so trifling a matter as the price of a native
Venus,
* * *
While two of the fleet of Peruvian slavers
appeared among the Elliee Group, the other
two remained to “ work ” Easter Island, the
which they did successfully, carrying away
all the able-bodied men and comely women
they could seize (300), to die miserably
in the guano-pits of the Chincha Islands.
The vessels which “ worked ” the Ellice Group
were a barque and a brig. The brig was
commanded by a big Irishman, and simply
because he was a big man and spoke in English to
the natives it was reported in the Hawaiian mis-
sionary press that the slaver captain was Bully.
The natives of Nukulaelae, an island which
suffered severely from the slavers’ visit, always
maintained for long afterward that it was Hayes,
whom they had never actually seen, becaus'e the
iki vaka (captain) was a tall, bearded man who
kept knocking his sailors down every minute if
they were not quick in their movements; and
didnt “make fast” ( i.c ., iron) any of the young
women.
* * #
But at the time the two Peruvians were
cruising through the Bllices, Bully was exploiting
the Paumotu Archipelago and arousing the anger
of the great Tahitian firm—the Maison Brander—
by his irregular business methods. For instance, he
would “buy” pearl-shell from their traders and
kick them over the side if they had the audacity to
ask for payment. In accordance with his custom,
Bully, on this cruise, devoted a good deal of time
to studying the soft-eyed Pauraotuan vahine; and
after filling his schooner with a fair amount of
plunder he did take away some ten or fourteen
young Paumotu women—not to Chili or Peru, but
merely on an extended and indefinite pleasure
trip. Most of these young ladies were desirous of
getting to Tahiti, where they believed their
charms would be better appreciated than in their
own island homes. In his characteristic way II
capitano [/(ilunluoino offered them free passages.
Passing through the Society Group and not enter-
ing Papeite Harbour (possibly on account of his
strained relations with the house of Brander and
the French naval authorities) he made his way to
the Marquesas. Here some four or five of his
lady passengers elected to remain with newly-
found lovers, either white or native ; and Bully
always blessed the union of two happy hearts by
recording the affair in his humorously-kept log
and giving a spree. If the bridegroom was a
white man, Bully would also “ buy ” his oil,
fungus and cotton, make him very drunk, place
his laughing and blushing bride in his arms, and
then, in his absent-minded way, see him over the
side into his boat and sail away without paying.
Bully used to say that his defective memory was
the cause of all the malignant slanders set afloat
about him. And, as regarded women, he used to
remark that he also suffered from the curious
complaint of “moral astigmatism.” The rest of
the girls reached home somehow, after undergoing
a pleasant and varied experience, each being the
happy possessor of one of his peculiar and char-
acteristically-written testimonials.
* # #
It was Bully’s humour to give these
precious documents to the time-expired members
of his harem, in the same manner as an English
mistress would give a certificate of character and
efficiency to a departing maid. Some of these
papers are still extant in Tahiti and Mangareva.
Many years ago when buying turtle at the little
island of Rurutu, I saw one pasted on a bread-
fruit post in a native house. In the Western
Carolines and the Pelew Group, when whale ships
were plentiful and prosperous, the native girls
preserved these “characters” by gumming the
paper (often upside-down) on a piece of pandanus
leaf bordered with devices in bead-work. When
a fresh ship arrived, the damsels would bind these
around their pretty little foreheads after the
manner of phylacteries—and they were always read
with deep interest by the blubber-hunting skippers
and mates and the after-guard generally. Bully’s
“characters ” ran somewhat in this wise :
TO ALL WHOM IT MAY CONCERN.
I, William H. Hayes, hereby certify that the bearer of
this, Marutahina, of Vahitahi, was with me for four or
five months, and I can confidently say that I can recom-
mend her to anyone in need of an active young wife,
general help, or to do chores. She is a very good girl
and the sole support of her mother—an old thief with a
tattooed back who lives on Reka Reka.
• * *
About 1871, while in the service of the
North Pacific Transportation Co. of San Fran-
cisco, I heard and read a good deal of Bully’s
doings. The Daily Alta used to speak of him as
a venturesome and high-spirited American gentle-
man upholding the honour of his flag in the South
Seas by disregarding the hateful tyranny of petty
British consuls; while the San Francisco Bulle-
tin called him a vile and brutal miscreant who
be hanged on the same gallows with Ala-
bama Semmes and Shenandoah Wardell —to whom,
it is interesting to remember, the Melbourne Club
gave a ball at which the adoring women cut off as
souvenirs the uniform buttons of the gallant pirate
and his officers. The spitfire Chronicle, edited
by Charles De Young (afterwards shot by Kal-
loch, jun.), “claimed” that Captain William
Henry Hayes was one of Nature’s gentlemen, and
was certainly not the cause of a terrible domestic
affliction that had befallen the editor of their
esteemed morning contemporary (the Bulletin
man’s wife had bolted with someone).
* * *
Leaving the Co.’s service with a good deal
of saved pay, I went to Tahiti in a coffin-barquen-
tine called the Constitution. I soon got a berth
as trader in the Gambler Islands, and, while there,
saw some young girls whom Hayes had bought
from the King of Aana (one of the Chain Islands).
I hey were very young, very scantily dressed, and,
without doubt, as beautiful as any Mexican girls
I ever saw in Southern California. They were
always shepherded, day and night, by two old
women. One of these chaperones, named Tuna
(the Eel), told me that, by-and-by, the “ big
captain ” would come and take them. This old
Madam Maquerelle Tuna had quite a fund of
anecdota about Bully, whom she regarded as im-
measurably superior to any white man she had
ever seen. When Tuna was a young and giddy
girl of sixteen, she had been much admired, so
she said, by Lord John Townshend (Te ariki
Tauseni) and the officers of His Majesty’s ship
Blossom (1828). Bully Hayes was Lord John’s
spirit returned in another and much stronger
body and better shape; and just as she had fallen
in love with the man-of-war captain, so had all
the Aana girls with his latter-day double.
• • #
At this time, I was the only white man on the
island, and Tuna and her long-haired, dark-eyed
“boarding-school ” came daily to my house, where
they sat on an upturned canoe and drank stone-
gm (luna took her’s neat), while teaching me to
pronounce properly the Paumotu language. Ye
gods ! what eyes those girls possessed ! What is
it —that soft glow of slumbering liquid fire one
sees in the women’s faces of quick-blooded races ?
Any one of those five island girls would have utterly
demoralised even a Trappist monastery, had the
holy brothers seen her face peeping in during
their orisons. This island, Nukutavake, had
but fovv inhabitants, most of whom had boon
brought there by Hayes, who, they said, would
come again in a year or so and take them back to
Aana and Maga-Reva. They were all political
offenders, and to escape death they besought Bully
to take them to Nukutavake until “ the wrath of
the chiefs was dead.” Bully, who had an idea that
there was a lot of pearl-shell on Nukutavake, gave
them all a passage, and also the two old women
and the girls before mentioned. One of the latter,
lalalua, told me that the captain would have taken
her with him, but the ship was too small and
he had no more room, and there were two girls
from Huaheine—dogs with much gold in their
ears with him, who threatened to give her to the
sharks if she came aboard. After I had spent
some months at Nukutavake, one of Brander’s
vessels came for me and I went to another island
called Vairaatea. My departure was marked by
the tearful farewells of Bully’s beauties and old
lima, who embraced me and rubbed noses, and
wept gin-odorous tears of unalloyed gratitude
when I gave her three bottles of liquor. To each
of Hayes’s nymphs I presented a piece of book-
muslin (12 yards each) and a bottle of musk valued
at two dollars a bottle. Talalua and Marami each
gave me some splendid pieces of hawk-bill shell,
and the others contributed among them a silver
ring. Poor girls ! they had no more to give—a
grass titi round their shapely loins and a few silver
or gold rings and ear pendants being all their
worldly wealth and clothing combined. I was
solemnly cautioned never to let Tapeni Esi (Cap-
tain Hayes) know about the turtle-shell and other
gifts or his anger would “eat them up.”
* * *
On hearing of this farewell testimonial busi-
ness, the skipper of Brander’s schooner became
greatly excited—Hayes, he said, would put his
own construction on my gifts. To this, in my
youthful innocence, I replied that X didn’t care, as
it was my business to make a present if I chose;
whereupon the skipper, a jolly old sea-dog named
Tom English, told me that that was all very well,
but that he (English) would be looked upon as an
accessory, and Hayes would make him suffer for
it when they met again.
“ Accessory to what ? ” I said.
“ D your thick skull, you voting ass, why
accessory to makin’ love to his girls.”
Which tickled me immensely, but as I saw that
English was serious, and I was equally determined
not to take the presents back, I wrote a note as
follows, and, after showing it to the old fellow, he
said it might possibly pass with Bully :
Capt. Hayes.
I)r. to , Agent for Maison Brander.
1 piece muslin, 12yds., per Talalua ... $3.00
J » i> >i ~ Marami ... $3.00
} » )> »• „ Sara $3.00
1 » )> » „ Louise ... $3.00
J. » .. ~ Tamarua ... $3.00
5 bottles scent (musk), at $l.OO $5.00

Capt. Hayes. Dear sir,—The above-mentioned I have
supplied as per bill. I will feel obliged if you will pay
the $20.00 to any of our firm’s vessels on my a/c. I hope
that, as I have not charged you native prices, you will
pay me soon.—Yours, &c., .
I handed the bill to old Tuna, and told her
that she must give it to the captain when he
reached Nukutavake. When I did meet Bully, a
long time afterwards, he paid up like a man, and
two hours afterwards won it back from me in
Charley Cook’s saloon in Apia. X may here men-
tion that old Tuna gave him my letter, and Hayes
wrote me the following epistle, which was lying at
the American Consulate at X 3 apeite, in Tahiti, for
nearly two years before I got it
Mr. ———. Dear sir,—l received your note and bill
for supplying some of my girls with some rotten chetse-
cloth out of Brander’s store, which you have the infernal
impertinence to call muslin ; also, 5 bottles of slinking
bilge-water, labelled musk. I don’t know who you are
but you can tell old Brander, from me, that I will see hint
roasted before I will give him my good money for his
filthy and disgusting Oahu trade goods ; and when I drop
across you, you will get a head put on you that-will teach
you not to meddle with my women, as it they were beach
girls.—Yours very sincerely, Wm. Henry Haves

A School-Teacher’s Idyl.
THE SLAPPING OF SAUNDERS.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Some people pin their faith to one crop; some to
another. Partly, of course, it is a question of
soil and climate. But wherever there is room for
it, you will see the play of predilection. One man
grows nothing but maize and potatoes; another
prefers wheat; a third, up North, says nothing
will pay like sugar. For my part, I pin my faith
to children. They are the best crop Australia
produces.
On the whole, the various Departments of Public
Instruction cultivate them very well. They create
teachers, male and female, in the image and like-
ness of the Ministerial ideal for the time being,
and send them forth to “whale” and to preach.
Perhaps the female teachers are the more attrac-
tive—to a male. You may discern differences in
the different provinces, but in each the teacher is
the better for knowledge which her sex does not
generally possess. Caught young and tender, she
is easily trained, and by the time she has a school
of her own she is worth knowing—if she has not
lost all her sap in the process. Those who, being
female teachers, remain females, are fit help-
meets for the best of men. Their intuition is
elevated by intelligence; their v. 1. bus are chastened
by discipline ; their acquaint;)u; o with capes and
mountains the lore of unman.
The things I have to tell happened in Queens-
land. They might just as well have happened
anywhere else; but they didn’t. It is better to
speak the truth.
A mixed school occurs in places where there are
not enough boys and girls to make it worth while
teaching them separately. Nowadays, mixed
schools are always placed in charge of a married
man. Not many people know the legend of the
last mixed school in Queensland taught by a single
man.
His name was Saunders, and he had “a down
on ” his assistant. There was no doubt of that.
Her name was Jane; and he would annoy her by
calling her in his petty, paltry, punning, peda-
gogic way “janeyus.” “My compliments to
Miss Janeyus,” he would say, “and ask her to
step here a moment.” The boy would repeat the
message literally, and Jane never failed to squirm.
But she always came. She had to. Her applica-
tions for transfer were fruitless.; her complaints
to the inspector wasted breath. After three
years’ torture she made up her mind to resign—
and changed it, hoping against hope.
Then the crisis arrived. She was giving a
sewing-lesson with which, of course, Saunders had
nothing to do; and he came to worry her. He
walked round sniffing and sneering. “ What on
earth’s this?” he suddenly cried, seizing a
sampler and holding it aloft ’twixt scornful finger
and thumb. Then he threw it down before the
child. She was the best girl in the class, and
seeing how affronted the mistrgss looked, she burst
into tears. Jane went as red as fire, then as white
as a ghost. Then she turned on Saunders, and
slapped his face—hard.
He and she cowered, expecting a
blow. The school stared breathless with ashen
faces. But he did not strike her; he did not even
speak. He controlled himself by a visible effort,
went back (■- h' n class, and was vety quietfor
therestof tin* .d -moon.
At four o’clock there was a message that Miss
Wilson was wanted in Mr. Saunders’ room. She
went. He placed a chair for her, and she sat
down.
“I suppose,” he said, formally, “that after
what has happened to-day you will wish to send
in your resignation at once?”
She bowed, and there was a pause. Venturing
to look up, she thought she saw a red mark on his
face, and hastily looked down again.
“It will prevent further unpleasantness,” he
said.
She could not trust herself to speak ; all the
energy had gone from her.
“If you will hand it to me in the morning,” he
said, “ I shall be glad to transmit it.”
She rose, and tottered to the door. He politely
held it open. She crossed the threshold, took a
step—and fainted.
He just saved her from falling, and she lay in
his arms unconscious. Her face was deadly pale.
He called loudly for water, but no one answered.
He cursed himself for his brutality, and prayed
her to recover. She was a long time coming round,
and when her eyes at last opened, he smothered
her with kisses, and she nearly fainted again. But
not quite.
When, a fortnight later, Saunders got a letter
from the department, saying that owing to the
representations of residents it had been decided to
establish a separate girls’ school at the beginning
of the year, he was able to write back and say
that he had arranged to marry Miss Wilson, his
assistant, and recommended her for the post of
head mistress.
Singularly enough, Saunders worships the
ground she treads on.
S.
What’s in a Name?
(FOR THE BULLETIN. )
Speaking of the gallantry, urbanity and many
gentlemanly qualities of Peter Jackson, the re-
nowned coloured pugilist, reminds me of an in-
teresting incident I once witnessed in a smoking-
carriage on a Southern line.
The carriage was one of those roomy affairs
with a single seat running right round and a
range of spittoons down the middle. Besides
myself, there were in the compartment three
bumptious “ drummers ” and a large, complacent,
neatly-dressed coloured gentleman, who lolled
back in his seat, and dreamily consumed a cigar.
For some time the commercials amused them-
f flves playing euchre on the end of a sample-box,
.nud juggling with three adult spirit-flasks, but in
a while their natural and carefully-cultivated
“ cheek ” developed into truculence, and a broad,
stiff man, with a port-wine stain and a feline
moustache, begin to “ pick at ” the nigger. He
told several of those charming little anecdotes
peculiar to the craft, the last of which reflected
seriously upon blacks in general, with a strong
inference towards the darkey in company parti-
cularly.
The latter appeared to pay no attention what-
ever to the talk of the drummers ; he lit another
cigar, spread himself all over the seat, and wore a
look of ingenuous felicity as the smoke rolled from
his capacious nostrils. Finding inference of no
avail, the man of commerce became more direct
and personal.
“Say, old man,” he said, “that's a good cigar
of yours. Got any more ?”
The sable stranger rolled his eyes slowly towards
the speaker, and surveyed him for a moment;
then he leisurely drew a cigar-case from his pocket,
opened it, and offered smokes all round. The
drummer was distinctly pulled back, but he had
set himself to do something smart, and so made
another try presently.
“I never yet came across a darkey who could
play euchre,” said he, flirting the pack ostenta-
tiously.
Once again the whites of the dark man’s eyes
began to revolve. He removed the cigar, knocked
the ash off with exaggerated deliberation, restored
it to his mouth, and then as deliberately drew a
neat case from his vest pocket. From the case he
took a genteel, enamelled card, which he tossed
on to the box in front of the drummers.
“ I play euchre now’n again,” he said ; “ that’s
my trump-card.”
The commercials examined the card. During
the next ten minutes three silent and dreadfully-
surprised men occupied the end seat of that car-
riage, and literally stewed in their own juice. At
Seymour they stepped off, but only to take a com-
partment up near the engine. The’ darkey
watched them as they went, and gradually Ids
face relaxed and his mouth expanded into a
copious grin.
I picked up the card that had dropped from the
drummer’s fingers. It was inscribed “P. Jack-
son.”
“ Why didn’t you give him a smack?” I asked.
“Most likely he’d a-licked. me,” answered Mr.
P. Jackson. “My name’s Paul Jackson. I can’t
fight for sour apples.”
Melb.
E.D.
The Reward of Politeness.
(FOR THE BULLETIN. )
He was strolling down that walk in Hyde Park,
Sydney, which leads towards Market-street tram
stopping-place. He saw something fall, and
looking up, observed a handsome young woman,
carrying a shawl, making for a tram. When he
reached the fallen article he found it to be a
teething-ring. You know the things I mean.
Humanity, in its crawling period, is deluded by
them into believing that cutting teeth is a pleasant
process. The tootn-ring was of no use to him, the
young woman was beautiful, the shawl must have
contained a baby.
Politeness, that is to say the outward and
visible expression of the quality called politeness,
chiefly consists of what in France is called
“ front.” No truly polite man, for instance, ever
picked up and publicly handed to a girl the flaring
red garteT she had dropped. This is but a side-
reflection.
The tram was now half-way to Park-street, yet
after it he went.
Trams are never very fast until someone
is chasing them. That young man thought
he’d caught it, but he hadn’t. The tram
might have been going still, with its panting
Sursuer in the rear, only for a block at the top of
•xford-street. Dashing along the cars he saw the
young woman, saw the shawl, but look for
the baby. The baby didn’t strike him as being
important. Between his struggles for breath he
gasped :
h our—baby’s—ring,” and he held it out.
Ihe young woman shrieked, as only voune
women can, and nearly fell out of the tram. 6
There wa»no baby !
Ihe guard came along and helped everyone
within reach to abuse that young man.
He d made an awful mistake, and “ cleared ” a (
quickly as possible. But how was he to get rib
of that ring ? He dropped it stealthily, but some-
one picked it up and handed it to'him with a
malevolent leer. Several times he endeavoured to
part company with it, but it was always restored
to him. He hadn’t the pluck to deny its owner-
ship, so at last, in desperation, he put it in his
coat-pocket.
When he got home, his mother found it there
and was shocked. The hope of her life had gone
astray ! Explanation was useless.
“ How do I know all this ?” you ask.
I was that young man.
PAUL LEGON.
Why?
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“The Push” had gone through a country
“bloke,” J
We are told in the Scripture story—
The way they mauled him it was no joke,
They blackened his eyes and his ribs they broke
(So runneth the legend hoary);
And with many an ancient Hebrew curse,
Which to point would defile my elegant verse,
They snatched his watch and snav'elled his purse
And left him bashed and gory.
So flat on the Jericho road, ’mid the stones,
His banged head throbbing and humming,
He lay with a shirtful of broken bones,
Girning his deep and dismal groans,
And crying for help in feeble tones
To the police, who were slow in coming.
A Levite came by that self-same day,
And a fat priest, sleek and ruddy—
They viewed the man as he moaning lay,
Stiff and sore and soiled with clay,
And battered and bruised and bloody ;
And they hurried by on the farther way—
Perchance they had not time to stay,
Or had a pastoral call to pay,
Or mayhap the road was muddy.
Now, why did that fat priest pass him by,
And that Levite, so sleek and steady ?
Why were they deaf to his dismal cry ?
Why did the good men draw not nign
To slobber o’er him, and pray, and sigh ?
The man had been robbed already I
Boorhaman, Yio.
J.M.W.

whose photo, appeals herewith; whereby she became

Mr. Harle; as a Mesmerist,

AUCKLAND.
“In Four Seasons.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN. )
Fomi times I saw her in as many years. The
first time was in Summer, and she, beneath a
gigantm fern tree by the creek, was washing
clothes. Her age, not 17. Her face with childish
innocence was sweet, as was her voice which
raised in song, mingled with the notes of whip-
bird, black-cap, and a hundred more. She was
happy. He had not yet come,
* * •
’Twas Autumn when I saw her once again. In
a theatre crowded hot and close. The healthy
colour of the bush had gone ; her dress was gay,
her face wore a charmed look. She was still
happy. He was by her side.
* * *
One wet, cold night in Winter was the third.
She stopped me in the street. The wreck of what
had once been innocent and sweet. But when she
saw my look of horror, she started, drew her thin
dress tightly round her form and hurried on
facing the driving rain. ’
* * *
In Spring, the last and happier than the third—
and yet 1 saw her not. Upon a marble slab a
something lay. A jet of water washed away
the river mud from out “its” golden hair. The
birds notes sounded by the creek at home. The
ferns and trees put forth fresh fronds and leaves
Enough the epitaph she had : “Found drowned.”
lor she had sought a greater change than is the
loveliness and warmth of Spring from cold and
cheerless Winter.
Vic.
THE PAUPER.
To Memory Dear.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A Queensland town, which need not here be
named,
Where ironbark woods do closely hug' the
clearing,
For showers of sand and leaves ’tis somewhat
tamed,
And there the red sun gets the biggest share in
Hemmed by a run where great king squatter
reigned—
The small selector generously sparing—
Although by greed impelled his ardent wish is
io bolt him as a shark would smaller fishes.
A string of straggling shanties built of bark,
With here and there a roof of sultry iron
1 hat stood above the rest as if to mark
greater from the smaller that environ
J ust as the parson stands above the clerk ’
T When preaching of the devil, piling fire on.
Just so each whited corrugated roof
Above the humbler bark ones stood aloof.
A broad, bleached, sandy stream-bed mid the flat
OaJJcd up the ghost of a departed river,
VV hereon the shimmer of the noon sun sat
_ lose again to cast a dazing Quiver
Between the bowed-down files of tea-trees, that
beeped praying to the great Almighty Giver
Hi ram His blissful water on the land—
I was mostly twenty feet beneath the sand.
A spanking line of coaches, four-horse power

GAMBUSINO.
The copper plate erected by Captain
1' linders at the head of Memory Cove, near Port
Lincoln b. A to tell future travellers of the fate
o Mr Hustle and the boat’s crew drowned near
Cape Catastrophe in 1 eb., 1802, is now in Adelaide

Here is an incident from the South
“—Mississippi, written in April, 1890,
just after the Grippe had visited that
country. ‘‘l am a farmer, one of
those who have to rise early and
work late. At the beginning of last
Winter I was on a trip to the City
of Vicksburg, Miss.,where I got well
drenchcyl in a shower of rain. I
went home and was soon after seized
with a -dry, hacking cough. This
grew worse every day, until I had
to seek relief. I consulted Dr. Dixon
who has since died, and he told me
to get a bottle of Boschee’s German
Syrup. t Meantime my cough grew
worse and worse and then the Grippe
came along and I caught that also
very severely. My condition t]jeu
compelled me to do something. I
got two bottles of German Syrup. I
began using them, and before taking
much of the second bottle, I was
entirely clear of the Cough that had
hung to me so long, the Grippe, and
all its bad effects. I felt tip-top and
have felt that way ever since.”
Pkter J. Brians, Jr., Cayuga, Hines
Co., Miss. ®

COLLEGE, MEDICAL SCHOOL-
Glasgow, 16. th Nov., 1892.
To Messrs. Bosisto and Co.,—
It is with pleasure that I write to state
my opinion of your Eucalyptus Oil. It is de-
cidedly better than any other similar oil which
can be obtainedjn this country.
It is a lighter, clearer and more ethereal oil
than any I have hitherto used, besides being free
from the heavy, nauseous odour characteristic of
others.
This is particularly noticeable when the oil is
sprayed into the throat.
It is very palatable when taken internally,
which places it above other preparations and con-
siderably enhances its value.
(Signed),
GEORGE BELL TODD, M. 8., Pro-
fessor of Zoology, Anderson’s Coll. :
Assistant Physician, Samaritan Hos-
pital for Diseases of Women, Glasgow.
The above is a most valuable testimonial. It
was entirely unsought, and was freely sent to
Bosisto and Co., of Richmond.
BOSISTO’S “PARROT BRAND”
EUCALYPTUS OIL is sold in all the colonies
at Is. per bottle.
Beware of inferior articles now offering. Many
of these so called “ eucalyptus oils ” are produced
from messmate and bastard gums, nauseous in
odour, and almost worthless from a medical point
of view.
Ask for Bosisto’s “Parrot Brand” Eucalyptus
Oil. Look for the words, “J. Bosisto, Rich-
mond,” blown on bottle. None other is genuine.

Old Death’s-head.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Our wardsman was morose, sullen, mysterious.
Warrego Jack, vainly struggling with phthisis,
swore he was mad. I christened him Old Deaths-
head.
Our side of ward B had eight beds, and eight
patients, I among the number, groaned in them.
We seemed a doomed lot, as first one and then
another went off, until only I and Ginger Wat-
son, a Barcoo man, remained.
Old Death’s-head was as merry as a marriage-
bell whenever a man “ turned it up,” as we used
to say. As he drew the screen around the cadaver,
he would whistle the while, and then, turning to
the man nearest, would say : “Your turn next.
Have the screen round you soon.” We grew to
hate him.
Watson at last died, and I alone remained.
“Good record!” muttered Old D. “Seven
gone—one more, and then I’m off, too. All dead
—dead as Julius Caesar !”
Once I awoke to find the doctor and two nurses
at my bedside.
“ Feel better ? ” asked the doctor.
“ Pretty weak. What’s up ?” I asked.
“ Lie still—how did you get the bandages off ? ”
“ Must have had the nightmare,” I thought.
A week passed and I gained strength rapidly.
“You’re going to slip through, after all,” said
Death’s-head. “Why don’t you die like the rest? ”
“ Get out! ” I replied. He laughed, muttered,
and moved on to wax the floor. Next morning,
bandages off again. I half dead, providentially
found bleeding to death in time. The doctor
talked of a strait waistcoat. The thing was a
mystery to me.
One night, as I grew stronger, I lay awake.
The ward was in darkness and silence, save for a
groan from the opposite side. Suddenly I heard a
stealthy step and felt the inexplicable feeling ever
attendant on a material presence. I was about to
speak, but a hand gently felt my face and I
refrained. Then the hand slid quietly down
beneath the bedclothes, and deftly, softly, the
bandages were being manipulated. This could be
done easily as my limb, mangled and torn by the
reaper, was slung in a wicker-work arrangement
clear of the bed. With the strength of rage and
fear combined I seized the wrist and held on
while I cried loudly for help. I was dragged to
the floor suffering tortures, but held on. Pre-
sently a rush of feet and lights, and as a flash
showed me the face of my ghostly visitant, I
muttered, “Old Death’s-head, by !” and
fainted. I eventually got well,
I don’t think they allow Old D. to attend the
sick patients in Callan Park Lunatic Asylum.
One of the results of the depression in
Queensland is that the number of boys doing
men’s work has been largely increased during the
past twelve months. In stores, in offices, in
factories, and everywhere, men are told to stand
aside and boys are put in their places. The press
is silent on this matter for the best of reasons.
Boys (“ improvers ” and apprentices) have ousted
many men because they are cheaper in the com-
posing-room.
After Solicitor-General Isaacs had leapt
over the heads of his legal “ seniors ” and offered
his tribute to Higinbotham on behalf of the Bar of
Victoria, he got a “nasty one” from junior
Purves. The smarting Q.O. observed that Isaacs
had left little for him to say, but he thought he
might venture to suppose that “if the shade of
the late Chief Justice were present nothing would
be more abhorrent to it than stilted eulogy .”
Purves was not quite right there, however. An
open display of ill-feeling between learned brethren
would have offended the good man’s spirit most of
all.
Dear Bulletin, —You seem to think that
camels are an absolute necessity in Western N.S.
Wales. I’d like you to name one track, from
Bourke to any part of N.S.W., that is impassable
for horse or bullock teams, even in the driest
seasons. Certainly, ere Government tanks and
watering places were constructed along the dry
roads, the camels would have been a boon. But
now their proper place is not N.S.W., but the
sandy wastes of Queensland, Centralia, and \Ves-
tralia, where they are almost indispensable. You
off handedly suggest that the horse might be
abolished in favour of the camel. So it will, when
N.S.W. is handed over to the Asiatics, which, at
the present rate of progress, will be sooner than
you expect. You say the fittest is going to sur-
vive. Well, the camel is not the fittest now in
N.S. Wales, whatever it might have been 20
years ago. Camels don’t take kindly to waggons ;
they are too-slow and stubborn, and thejr feet
were not designed for heavy pulling, and if you
advocate the present system of camel-loading you
advocate barbarous cruelty in many cases—in
carrying wool, wire-netting, boring-pipes, and
timber, all of which the poor brutes have to
struggle under. The constant friction causes
frightful sores on their backs, which their kind
masters, the Afghans, sometimes endeavour to
hide from the public gaze by sewing basil or can-
vass on to the live skin; but, as a rule, they don’t
bother about it at all, as they aren’t liable for
cruelty to animals, the camel not being an animal
“ within the meaning of the Act,” according to our
local magistrate. For the same reason they have full
possession of the Bourke Common free of charge,
whereas carriers and others have to pay stiffly for
having their horses scattered in all directions. If,
as you seem to wish, the camel gets possession of
the roads, half the public watering-places will be
non-revenue-producing, for the wily Afghans use
the camel’s great powers of endurance to escape
payintr water charges. Also, If you were com-
pelled to live on some of the flour that is carried
on that smellful animal’s back, your enthusiasm
would cool considerably. Supposing there is any-
thing to be gained by substituting the camels for
horses and bullocks, how is it to be done, when
there are more teams now than there is employ-
ment for? Scores of teams are either camped, or
travelling, waiting or looking for something to do.
That being so, how, in the name of common sense,
are the white carriers going to sell their teams and
expensive waggons in an already overstocked
market, and buy camels at from .£4O to .£7O each ?
The thing is absurd, especially when there is no
benefit to be gained, but a decided loss, to this
district, which is the most important carrying dis-
trict in N.S.W.—P. A., Bourke (N.S.W.), 28th
Jan., 1893.
